201 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Advisory Opinion on Constitutionality of 1982 PA 47


Docket No. 69345


Supreme Court of Michigan


418 Mich. 49; 340 N.W.2d 817; 1983 Mich. LEXIS 257


March 8, 1983, Argued  
November 23, 1983, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action seeking an advisory opinion on the constitutionality of 1982 Mich. Pub. Acts 47, a voter-approved long-term borrowing act (Act), petitioner attorney general argued in opposition to the constitutionality of the Act, in which the remaining money of general obligation bonds authorized by the Act were to be issued at a rate of interest higher than the six percent authorized in the Act.


OVERVIEW: The court determined that the Act was constitutional until the contrary was shown. As such, the court held that the people, in answering in the affirmative the three-part ballot question on the 1968 Mich. Pub. Acts 76 approved the borrowing, or the three particulars of the borrowing required by Mich. Const. art. 9, § 15 to be set forth in the ballot question, and not the whole act. Additionally, the court held that even if the people were deemed to have approved the whole act, the legislature could nevertheless amend without voter approval an act authorizing long-term borrowing unless the amendment related to one or more of the three particulars, the amount to be borrowed, the specific purpose to which the funds were to be devoted, or the method of repayment identified in Mich. Const. art. 9, § 15. Therefore, the court held that the legislature could, without a vote of the people, amend voter approved legislation and, further, held that inferring a constitutional limitation would not implement or enforce the purpose and spirit of Mich. Const. art. 9, § 15, but would simply encumber the legislative process.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the Act was constitutional.

 

202 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Turmon


Docket No. 69776


Supreme Court of Michigan


417 Mich. 638; 340 N.W.2d 620; 1983 Mich. LEXIS 261


June 8, 1983, Argued  
November 21, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 117 Mich App 345; 323 NW2d 698 (1982).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed defendant's conviction for possession of a drug classified as a controlled substance by the Michigan Board of Pharmacy (board).


OVERVIEW: The board, pursuant to the authority granted to it under Mich. Comp. Laws § 333.7215 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 14.15(7215)), classified the drug that defendant possessed as a controlled substance. Defendant contended on appeal of his conviction that the statute lacked sufficient standards to govern the exercise of the delegated power and represented an unconstitutional attempt by the legislature to delegate its exclusive power to create crimes, the board abused its authority, and he was denied proper notice that his conduct was criminal. The intermediate appellate court agreed and reversed the conviction, holding that the legislature improperly delegated authority to the board and that defendant was not given notice that possession of the drug was a crime. On further appeal, the court reversed, holding that: (1) the legislature's delegation of authority was not an unlawful delegation of power despite the fact that penal consequences flowed from violation of the board's rules; (2) the statute contained sufficient safeguards to avoid infirmity under both separation of powers and due process challenges; and (3) the board did not abuse its discretion in the promulgation of the rule.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the intermediate appellate court's decision to overturn defendant's conviction for possession of a controlled substance.

 

203 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Coles


Docket Nos. 60641, 60893, 62938


Supreme Court of Michigan


417 Mich. 523; 339 N.W.2d 440; 1983 Mich. LEXIS 248


March 10, 1983, Argued  
October 24, 1983, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at People v. Coles, 136 Mich. App. 7, 355 N.W.2d 686, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 2810 (1984)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 79 Mich App 255; 261 NW2d 280 (1977).

 Cummins v People, 42 Mich 142; 3 NW 305 (1879), overruled.
People v. Gonzales, 412 Mich. 917, 1982 Mich. LEXIS 1322 (1982)
People v. Waits, 412 Mich. 914, 1982 Mich. LEXIS 1320 (1982)
People v. Coles, 79 Mich. App. 255, 261 N.W.2d 280, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 861 (1977)



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed, but the cases are remanded to the Court of Appeals for reconsideration of the sentences.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, in a consolidated action, challenged the respective judgments of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed their convictions and sentences. The court granted leave to appeal and directed the parties to include among the issues to be briefed whether the court should adopt a procedure for appellate review of criminal sentences and, if necessary, reconsider the court's decision in Cummins v People.


OVERVIEW: Defendants challenged the intermediate appellate court's affirmation of their convictions and sentences. The court overruled Cummins v People, 42 Mich. 142; 3 N.W. 305 (1879), in which the court held that it had no authority to review statutorily approved sentences other than for whether or not they were cruel and unusual punishment. The court examined constitutional and statutory authority and concluded that appellate courts had jurisdiction to review all sentencing issues. The court held that prospectively it would review otherwise lawful sentences on whether or not the trial court had abused its discretion, and defined abuse of discretion as that which shocked the conscience of the appellate court. The court directed that in the future trial courts should consider (1) the disciplining of the wrongdoer, (2) the protection of society, (3) the potential for reformation of the offender, and (4) the deterring of others from committing like offenses in imposing a sentence, and that they must state on the record what criteria were considered and what reasons supported their decisions in order to allow appellate review. Defendants' other alleged errors were dismissed as without merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court as to defendants' convictions, dismissing all alleged errors as being without merit. However, the court, reversed prior case law and held that it had jurisdiction to review all sentencing issues. Therefore, each case was remanded for reconsideration of defendants' sentences in light of the new standards of review by either the intermediate appellate or trial courts.

Board of Education v. Parks


Docket Nos. 65818, 65819, 65820, 65821


Supreme Court of Michigan


417 Mich. 268; 335 N.W.2d 641; 1983 Mich. LEXIS 234; 114 L.R.R.M. 3269


March 8, 1983, Argued  
June 27, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 98 Mich App 22; 296 NW2d 815 (1980).
Detroit Bd. of Education v. Parks, 98 Mich. App. 22, 296 N.W.2d 815, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2719 (1980)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tenured teachers challenged a decision from the court of appeals (Michigan) in favor of appellee employer and union holding that a tenured teacher could be discharged for failing to pay agency service fees under a contract to that effect, and that the teacher tenure act was both substantively and procedurally inapplicable.


OVERVIEW: The court held that neither the substantive nor the procedural provisions of the teacher tenure act applied when a teacher was discharged, pursuant to a contractual authorizing provision, for failure to pay agency fees. The court interpreted the phrase "condition of employment" in the Public Employment Relation Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 423.210(1)(c), under the guidance of the National Labor Relations Act, 29 U.S.C.S. § 151 et seq. The court concluded that teachers' employment could be conditioned on payment of agency service fees and a union and employer could validly agree that discharge was a permissible remedy against employees who failed to pay agency service fees.


OUTCOME: Decision affirmed in part and remanded.

 

205 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Kelley v. Riley


Docket No. 70876


Supreme Court of Michigan


417 Mich. 119; 332 N.W.2d 353; 1983 Mich. LEXIS 230


January 24, 1983, Argued  
February 11, 1983, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Decided on Reconsideration February 15, 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case of first impression, after defendant supreme court justice was appointed to fill a court vacancy, plaintiff state attorney general filed a quo warranto action, which bypassed the appellate court, for an interpretation of Mich. Const. art. 6, § 23,.


OVERVIEW: A state supreme court justice was reelected but died before the expiration of the current term. The governor appointed the new justice to finish the decedent's current term and to fill the upcoming term. The state filed a quo warranto action and contended that under Mich. Const. art. 6, § 23,, the appointment of the justice was limited to the current term and that the appointment could not extend into the new term. The court held that Mich. Const. art 6, § 23,, could operate only within the Supreme Court justice term in which the vacancy arose. Therefore, the Governor was authorized only to appoint until the end of the current eight-year term.


OUTCOME: The court construed the constitution and held that the appointment of the justice was limited to the term in which the vacancy occurred.

 

206 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Silva v. Ada


Docket Nos. 65815, 66201


Supreme Court of Michigan


416 Mich. 153; 330 N.W.2d 663; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 618


January 6, 1982, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 99 Mich App 601; 298 NW2d 838 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners appealed judgments from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which found for defendants, townships, and individuals, and upheld zoning ordinances that prohibited mining of natural resources. The landowners contended that it was error to sustain the ordinances in the absence of findings that "very serious consequences" would result from the mining.


OVERVIEW: In these consolidated cases, landowners challenged the reasonableness of zoning ordinances which denied them the ability to take gravel and silica sand from their respective properties. In reversing the judgments and remanding the causes, the court held that the lower court erred in failing to determine that "very serious consequences" would result from the exploitation of the resources. "Very serious consequences" was the court's articulation of the heightened standard of reasonableness that applied to zoning laws which would deny a landowner the right to extract minerals or other natural resources from its property. The court held that the rule retained its vitality despite the lower court's apparent belief that it had been overruled sub silentio.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments that upheld the zoning ordinances against the landowners' challenges and remanded the causes for further proceedings.

 

207 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Tobin v. Michigan Civil Service Com.


Docket No. 65596


Supreme Court of Michigan


416 Mich. 661; 331 N.W.2d 184; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 630


January 6, 1982, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 98 Mich App 604; 296 NW2d 320 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff civil service employees appealed a judgment of the court of appeals (Michigan), which reversed the circuit court's grant of summary judgment for employees against defendant state agencies in employees' action under the Freedom of Information Act (FOIA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 15.231 et seq., Mich. Stat. Ann. § 4.1801(1) et seq.


OVERVIEW: Civil service employees filed suit against state agencies to enjoin disclosure of public records to labor organizations seeking to unionize state civil service employees. The employees argued that if a public record was one of those named in the FOIA as exempt from disclosure, the statute affirmatively prohibited disclosure. The circuit court granted employees' motion for summary judgment, but the appeals court reversed. On appeal, the court held that the FOIA authorized, but did not require, nondisclosure of public records falling within an FOIA exemption. Further, the names and addresses sought by the labor organizations were not ordinarily personal, intimate, or embarrassing pieces of information and there was nothing objectionable about the method by which the information was obtained or proposed to be released.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals reversal of the circuit court's grant of summary judgment to employees in their suit to enjoin release of information under the FOIA.

 

208 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Williams


Docket No. 64639


Supreme Court of Michigan


416 Mich. 25; 330 N.W.2d 823; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 610


January 7, 1981, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 95 Mich App 1; 289 NW2d 863 (1980).
People v. Williams, 95 Mich. App. 1, 289 N.W.2d 863, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2425 (1980)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the defendants' convictions are reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted of first-degree criminal sexual conduct, Mich. Comp. Laws §750.520b (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.788(2)). Defendants challenged Michigan's rape shield law, Mich. Comp. Laws §750.520j (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.788(10)). The Court of Appeals reversed their convictions, holding the statute violated the right of effective cross-examination protected by the Confrontation Clause of U.S. Const. amend. VI, and the state appealed.


OVERVIEW: At trial, defendants sought to present evidence that the complainant had sex with one of them before, and that she was a prostitute. The trial judge precluded the admission based on defendants' failure to comply with the rape shield law's notice provision. The court of appeals held the notice provision of § 520j unconstitutional when applied to preclude evidence of specific instances of sexual conduct between the complainant and one defendant. Without deciding whether § 520j embodied a mandatory preclusion sanction if its notice requirement was not met, or whether such a requirement could withstand constitutional scrutiny, the court held that the evidence sought to be admitted was irrelevant to the asserted defense of consent. The court held that the fact of sexual intercourse between the complainant and one defendant alone should not, without more, serve as substantive evidence that the complainant would consent to any type of group sexual encounter. As with prior sexual conduct evidence, and under Mich. R. Evid. 609, the court saw no logical relation between the complainant's reputation for prostitution and truthfulness. The convictions were reinstated.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and defendants' convictions were reinstated. The court did not rule whether the rape shield law was constitutional.

 

209 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Arenda


Docket Nos. 65578, 65579


Supreme Court of Michigan


416 Mich. 1; 330 N.W.2d 814; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 607


June 3, 1981, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 417 Mich 1105.


PRIOR HISTORY: 97 Mich App 678; 296 NW2d 143 (1980).
People v. Arenda, 97 Mich. App. 678, 296 N.W.2d 143, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2701 (1980)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed defendant's conviction for criminal sexual conduct in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for criminal sexual conduct with an eight-year-old boy. At trial, defendant sought to introduce evidence of sexual conduct between the victim and another person other than defendant. The trial court granted the state's motion to exclude such evidence based on the rape-shield statute set forth at Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.520j (Mich. Stat. Ann. 28.788(10)). On appeal, the court reversed the decision of the appellate court, holding that the rape-shield statute was constitutional and not violative of defendant's right of confrontation. Thus the court held that there was no error requiring reversal of defendant's conviction. The court found that the prohibitions contained in the rape-shield statute were rationally related to the protection of legitimate expectations of privacy and the prevention of the unfair prejudicing and misleading of the jury. Further, the court found that the prohibitions did not deny defendant his right of confrontation because the evidence acquired was minimally relevant. The court also held that the evidence sought could be obtained by other means without inquiry into the victim's sexual conduct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment from the appellate court reversing defendant's conviction for criminal sexual conduct in the first degree.

 

210 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Shafou


Docket No. 62885


Supreme Court of Michigan


416 Mich. 113; 330 N.W.2d 647; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 624


January 10, 1980, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 417 Mich 1113.


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Shafou, 406 Mich. 1012, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 979 (1979)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals is affirmed by an equally divided Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from a judgment of the trial court (Michigan), which reversed defendant's conviction for inciting, inducing, or exhorting the burning of real property in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.157b; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.354(2).


OVERVIEW: Defendant solicited a police informant to burn a competitor's store. On the day before the fire was to occur, defendant's brother telephoned the police informant and told him not to burn the store. Defendant was charged with and convicted of inciting, inducing, and exhorting the police informant to unlawfully burn in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws 750.157b; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.345(2). The appellate court reversed defendant's conviction on the ground that the trial court erred in refusing to instruct the jury that withdrawal was a defense. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and reinstated defendant's conviction, because no overt act by anyone other than defendant was necessary to convict under the statute. In addition, the court determined that withdrawal or abandonment was not a defense to the charge brought against defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court that reversed defendant's conviction for inciting, inducing, and exhorting another person to unlawfully burn property, and reinstated the trial court's decision to convict the defendant of the charge that was brought against him by the state.

 

211 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Esters


Docket No. 64265


Supreme Court of Michigan


417 Mich. 34; 331 N.W.2d 211; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 626


June 8, 1982, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Esters, 412 Mich. 864, 1981 Mich. LEXIS 1446 (1981)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgments below are affirmed by an equal division of the Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed his conviction from the trial court for armed robbery and the possession of a firearm during the commission of a felony. Defendant claimed that a pistol admitted into evidence was the result of an unlawful search of his car, that it was reversible error to admit his statement of noninvolvement, and that he was denied effective assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: The police were notified of a robbery and were given a license number and description of the robber. The police found the car, a man asleep in bed, and searched his car for a gun and his accomplice. Defendant requested an attorney but two days later, consented to give a statement of noninvolvement. The trial court denied the motion to suppress the gun and the statement. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court did not address the lawfulness of defendant's arrest because it was raised for the first time on appeal. The court found that the gun was properly admitted under the Fourth Amendment. The police had probable cause to search the car given the description of the robber and that the robbery had just occurred. The automobile exception applied as the car was easily moved and the police had reason to believe an accomplice was at large. Defendant's statement of noninvolvement voluntarily made to the police was properly used for impeachment purposes. Defendant was not denied effective assistance of counsel when his attorney performed at least as well as an attorney with ordinary skill in criminal law. He was not deprived of a substantial alibi defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for armed robbery and the possession of a firearm during the commission of a felony.

People v. Woods


Docket Nos. 63099, 65263, 65342


Supreme Court of Michigan


416 Mich. 581; 331 N.W.2d 707; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 632


December 2, 1981, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided  
January 25, 1983, Opinion Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 417 Mich 1113 as to Alexander and Woods. Certiorari denied by the Supreme Court of the United States on June 20, 1983 as to Alexander.
Writ of certiorari denied Michigan v. Alexander, 462 U.S. 1134, 103 S. Ct. 3116, 77 L. Ed. 2d 1370, 1983 U.S. LEXIS 640 (1983)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Tucker, 411 Mich. 901, 1981 Mich. LEXIS 1301 (1981)
People v. Alexander, 411 Mich. 899, 1981 Mich. LEXIS 1355 (1981)



DISPOSITION: Woods and Tucker affirmed. Alexander reversed and remanded for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two defendants (first-degree murder defendants) appealed the judgment of a trial court (Michigan), which convicted them of first-degree murder. The remaining defendant (second-degree murder defendant) appealed her conviction for second-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: The first-degree murder defendants and the second-degree murder defendant contended that the trial court erroneously used the terms "malice" and "malice aforethought" in its murder instructions to the jury. On appeal, the court held that: (1) with regard to the first-degree murder defendants, the trial court erred in instructing the jury that the law would imply malice from an unprovoked, inexcusable, unjustifiable killing; however, the error was harmless where all of the evidence indicated the existence of express malice rather than implied malice; (2) with regard to the second-degree murder defendant, the trial court's error in instructing the jury that one is presumed to intend the natural consequences of his or her acts was not harmless because the error involved the material element of intent which was directly disputed; and (3) the elements of murder were required to be described without the use of the terms "malice" or "malice aforethought."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the first-degree murder defendants' convictions. However, the court reversed the second-degree murder defendant's conviction and remanded the cause for a new trial.

 

213 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. Sheriff of Wayne County


Docket Nos. 61875, 62006


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


415 Mich. 658; 330 N.W.2d 335; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 601


October 3, 1979, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Brown v. Wayne County, 406 Mich. 892, 1978 Mich. LEXIS 704 (1978)
People ex rel. Wayne Prosecutor v. Recorder's Court Judge (In re Blount), 406 Mich. 892, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 828 (1979)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, two escaped convicts that were the subject of warrants for arrest and extradition based on a request by the governor of the demanding state, separately sought review of a Court of Appeals (Michigan) decision, which denied their requests for habeas corpus relief. The convicts claimed that prison conditions in the demanding state constituted cruel and unusual punishment in violation of the Eighth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The convicts testified that the prison conditions in the demanding state were life threatening. In deciding two consolidated class actions filed on behalf of inmates in the prisons of the demanding state, a federal district court in the demanding state found that prison conditions in that state constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The court affirmed and held that (1) the court was obliged by case law to hold that a review of prison conditions in the demanding state was limited in a habeas corpus action in the asylum state; (2) the right of an individual human being to be free from cruel and unusual punishment was outweighed by the importance of comity, full faith and credit to a sister state, and avoiding "balkanization" of the administration of criminal justice; and (3) in the hope that the court incorrectly interpreted U.S. Supreme Court case law, the execution of the extradition warrants were stayed until the time for filing a petition for certiorari had elapsed or such a petition was denied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's denial of the convicts' petition for habeas corpus relief.

 

214 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ford


Docket Nos. 64572, 64965, 65235


Supreme Court of Michigan


417 Mich. 66; 331 N.W.2d 878; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 625


April 8, 1981; June 3, 1981, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Gonzales, 409 Mich. 845, 1980 Mich. LEXIS 1498 (1980)
People v. Ford, 95 Mich. App. 412, 291 N.W.2d 60, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2476 (1980)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In Ford, reversed. In Gonzales and Howard, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate actions, defendants were convicted of uttering and publishing and larceny within a building, respectively. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed the uttering and publishing conviction on the ground that charging under a general statute when a specific charge existed was an abuse of prosecutorial discretion; it affirmed the larceny within a building convictions. Plaintiff State and defendants filed appeals, which were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: Defendants contended that it was an abuse of prosecutorial discretion for the State to charge them with general violations when more specific statutes were applicable. The court held that where a felony statute and a misdemeanor statute both included particular misconduct, the prosecutor had discretion to charge a defendant under the felony statute rather than the misdemeanor statute. Specifically, it found that the Michigan credit card statutes, Mich. Comp. Laws §§ 750.157m-.157u (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.354(13)-(20)), did not preclude charging an accused with uttering and publishing when a credit card slip was forged. The court reasoned that the prosecutor had discretion to charge under either the credit card statutes or the uttering and publishing statute because the knowing presentation of a forged writing was a prerequisite element of uttering and publishing which was not required to support a conviction under the credit card statutes. It further held that it was not an abuse of prosecutorial discretion to charge the felony of larceny in a builidng although the misdemeanor of general larceny might have been charged.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment as to one defendant and reinstated that defendant's conviction for uttering and publishing; it affirmed the lower court's judgment, which affirmed convictions for larceny within a building, as to the other defendants.

 

215 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carter


Docket No. 64583


Supreme Court of Michigan


415 Mich. 558; 330 N.W.2d 314; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 598


March 3, 1981, Argued  
December 23, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 417 Mich 1105.


PRIOR HISTORY: 94 Mich App 501; 290 NW2d 46 (1979).
People v. Carter, 94 Mich. App. 501, 290 N.W.2d 46, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 2553 (1979)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), attacking his joint convictions of aiding and abetting the commission of extortion and conspiracy to perpetrate extortion on the grounds of double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that defendant was properly convicted under both the aiding and abetting and conspiracy statutes. In assessing the applicability of Wharton's Rule, the target offense to be evaluated was the underlying substantive crime of extortion. Extortion was not a crime of such a nature as to necessarily require the participation of two persons. Therefore, defendant's prosecution and conviction for conspiracy to commit extortion did not violate Wharton's Rule. In addition, any presumption in favor of the application of Wharton's Rule was rebutted by the legislative intent expressed in the aiding and abetting statute. Further, the crimes were different for double jeopardy purposes under a necessarily lesser-included-offense analysis. Although the crimes contained common elements, aiding and abetting the commission of a crime and conspiracy to commit that crime were legally distinct offenses. The crimes were factually and theoretically independent, were neither inseparably intertwined nor merely alternative routes for creating liability for the substantive crime, and neither crime necessarily supplied an indispensable element of the other.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision.

School Dist. v. Kent County Tax Allocation Bd.


Docket No. 63962


Supreme Court of Michigan


415 Mich. 381; 330 N.W.2d 7; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 592


January 8, 1981, Argued  
December 22, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 417 Mich 1104.
Rehearing denied by EAST GRAND RAPIDS SCH. DIST. v. KENT COUNTY TAX ALLOCATION BD., 417 Mich. 1104, 1983 Mich. LEXIS 1538 (1983)


PRIOR HISTORY: 92 Mich App 786; 285 NW2d 463 (1979).

 315 Mich 566; 24 NW2d 213 (1946) overruled.
School Dist. v. Kent County Tax Allocation Bd., 92 Mich. App. 786, 285 N.W.2d 463, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 2396 (1979)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant statutory tax board sought review from a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the trial court's grant to plaintiff school district a writ of mandamus that directed the statutory tax board to make the findings and determinations required by the Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.251 et seq. for purposes of establishing a so-called variable millage.


OVERVIEW: The statutory tax board argued that Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.251 et seq., was unconstitutional because it violated Mich. Const. art. 9, § 3, which required that all general ad valorem property taxes be levied uniformly. The court agreed with the statutory tax board and noted that the statute at issue failed to harmonize with the constitution. The court pointed out that to satisfy the uniformity requirement of Mich. Const. art. 9, § 3 (1963), the tax rate imposed by the school district was required to be identical throughout the territory to which it applied. The statute that authorized variable rates of taxation within a single school district failed to do this and, thus, was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appeal's court judgment and overruled a prior decision of the court pertaining to the issue before the court. The court remanded to the trial court for entry of an order designed to refund or credit taxpayers for such sums as were improperly assessed pursuant to the unconstitutional act that allowed the assessment of variable millages.

 

217 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Citizens for Pretrial Justice v. Goldfarb


Docket No. 63739


Supreme Court of Michigan


415 Mich. 255; 327 N.W.2d 910; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 575


October 7, 1980, Argued  
December 20, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 88 Mich App 519; 278 NW2d 653 (1979).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals is modified as to the definition of the class of plaintiffs, affirmed as to the issue of excessive fees, reversed as to the issue of requiring collateral, and vacated as to the constitutional issue; and the case is remanded to the Wayne Circuit Court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant bail bondsmen appealed a decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the judgment finding that they violated Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.167b(3), pertaining to the fee charged for bonds, and plaintiff individuals appealed the redefinition of their class and the reversal of the order that found § 765.26 unconstitutional as violative of due process.


OVERVIEW: A citizens group and individuals brought a class action alleging that bail bondsmen violated § 750.167b(3) by exceeding the statutory maximum allowed for a bail bond fee and requiring collateral in addition to that fee. They also challenged the constitutionality of § 765.26, which provided authority to revoke a bond and re-arrest the principal. The trial court certified the class and granted judgment in its favor. On appeal the class was redefined and the order finding the bail bondsmen in violation of § 750.167b(3) was affirmed, but the order finding § 765.26 unconstitutional was reversed. Both sides appealed and the court modified the redefinition of the class by limiting it to those persons actually overcharged within six years prior to the action pursuant to § 600.5813. The court found that the bail bondsmen had overcharged for fees pursuant to the plain language of the statute, which left no room for judicial construction. The court found nothing in § 750.167b(3) that precluded the bail bondsmen from requiring collateral in addition to the fee charged. The court vacated the judgment on the constitutionality issue, finding that none of the individuals had standing to raise it.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order that redefined the class eligible to bring the action, affirmed that the bail bondsmen violated the statute maximizing the fee that could be charged for a bail bond, reversed the order that precluded the bail bondsmen from requiring collateral in addition to the fee charged, and vacated the decision concerning the constitutionality of the statute allowing for bond revocation and re-arrest of the principal.

 

218 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Consumers Power Co. v. Michigan Public Service Com.


Docket Nos. 62883, 62884


Supreme Court of Michigan


415 Mich. 134; 327 N.W.2d 875; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 582


January 9, 1980, Argued  
December 8, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 417 Mich 1103.


PRIOR HISTORY: 88 Mich App 633; 278 NW2d 702 (1979).
Consumers Power Co. v. Michigan Public Service Com., 88 Mich. App. 633, 278 N.W.2d 702, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 2010 (1979)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state public service commission, state attorney general, and retailers association, challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the trial court's decision granting appellee power company preliminary injunctive relief authorizing it to implement the proposed increased rate schedules under bond subject to refund of any excess collected.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that, under the circumstances of the case, including that the public service commission did not regard itself as having the authority to provide immediate relief, the trial court acted properly in entering an injunctive order. The court noted that the public service commission's subsequent action making the proposed rate structure effective as of the date that the injunction became effective confirmed the power company's right to retain substantially all the money collected pursuant to the injunctive order. The court further noted that the public service commission was of the opinion that the statute did not authorize it to grant immediate relief. In those circumstances, the court held that the trial court, sitting as a court of equity, could fill the procedural gap and enter a protective order subject to the public service commission's ultimate determination whether relief, immediate or permanent, was warranted. The court further concluded that the trial court had a sufficient factual basis upon which to premise injunctive relief and, therefore, the trial court did not err in granting the preliminary injunction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.

Bott v. Commission of Natural Resources etc.


Docket Nos. 60947, 62877, 62878


Supreme Court of Michigan


415 Mich. 45; 327 N.W.2d 838; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 581


January 8, 1980, Argued  
December 8, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 88 Mich App 120; 276 NW2d 538 (1979).
Bott v. Comm'n of Natural Res., 406 Mich. 1006, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 1033 (1979)
Nicholas v. McDaniel, 88 Mich. App. 120, 276 N.W.2d 538, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 1953 (1979)



DISPOSITION: The decision in Bott is affirmed. The decision in Nicholas is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, land owners and state natural resources officials, challenged a judgment of the court of appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the circuit court's grant of summary judgment to appellees, land owner and littoral owners, in the respective land owners' actions to have creeks adjoining lakes declared not navigable and, therefore, private.


OVERVIEW: Landowners owned the majority of land around two lakes and sought to have the lakes and adjoining creeks declared private because the creeks were not navigable, and there were no other means of access to the lakes. Littoral owners and department of natural resources sought public access through the creeks. The court of appeals affirmed the circuit court's finding that one lake was private and the other was not because its creek was navigable. On appeal, the court held that the creeks at issue were not of sufficient depth to be used for recreational purposes pursuant to the log-flotation test. Further, as creating a servitude for public access to inland waters created highways of commerce, the dead-end lakes surrounded by property whose owner would not permit a boat to dock could not be used as a highway. There was no showing that other public lakes, rivers, and streams did not meet public needs nor that the recreational boating test would increase the recreational opportunities of those who are unable to use the present waterways without eliminating property rights without compensation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed summary judgment finding one lake and creek to be private property and reversed summary judgment finding the other lake to be public and its creek to be navigable. The court stated that it was not an appropriate forum for resolving this case.

Kestenbaum v. Michigan State University


Docket No. 65473


Supreme Court of Michigan


414 Mich. 510; 327 N.W.2d 783; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 572


October 14, 1981, Argued  
December 7, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 417 Mich 1103.


PRIOR HISTORY: 97 Mich App 5; 294 NW2d 228 (1980).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals was affirmed by an equally divided Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mailer filed an action to determine whether defendants, university and its freedom of information officer, violated the Freedom of Information Act (FOIA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 15.231 et seq., Mich. Stat. Ann. § 4.1801(1) et seq., by denying the mailer's request for a copy of the magnetic tape used to produce the university's student directory. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) held that such denial was proper. The mailer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mailer contended that he was entitled to the tape under the FOIA and stated that he wanted the tape to facilitate political mailings in connection with an upcoming election. The university asserted that its position was supported by the FOIA. The court affirmed the judgment. In so doing, the court held that (1) the university was justified in denying the mailer's request because the release of the magnetic tape containing the names and addresses of students constituted a "clearly unwarranted invasion of an individual's privacy" within the meaning of § 13(1)(a), Mich. Comp. Laws § 15.243(1)(a); Mich. Stat. Ann. § 4.1801(13)(1)(a), (2) the mailer's intended use was irrelevant for purposes of the balancing test analysis, and (3) the mailer's argument that the university sacrificed any privacy interests that may have existed by making the information available in a printed directory was without merit because after weighing the public interest in disclosure against the potential invasion of privacy, the university was correct in concluding that the release of names and addresses on magnetic tape was a more serious invasion of privacy than disclosure in printed directory form.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Burnett v. Adrian

Therefore, the court held that the grant of summary judgment was improper.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment and remanded the cause for further proceedings.

Johnson v. Harnischfeger Corp.


Docket Nos. 64682, 64689


Supreme Court of Michigan


414 Mich. 102; 323 N.W.2d 912; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 549


January 7, 1981, Argued  
September 13, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 95 Mich App 380; 289 NW2d 919 (1980).
Johnson v. Harnischfeger Corp., 95 Mich. App. 380, 289 N.W.2d 919, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2471 (1980)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a former employee of defendant company who applied for workers' compensation based upon his loss of industrial use of his legs, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which determined that Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.361(2)(g) violated equal protection and due process and remanded his claim to the Workers' Compensation Appeal Board.


OVERVIEW: The employee and received 500 weeks of workers' compensation benefits for total incapacity from injuries to his right knee. Five weeks after payments ceased, he filed a petition for hearing and alleged total and permanent disability resulting from the loss of industrial use of his legs pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.361(2)(g). He was found disabled, and the Board reversed; the court of appeals reversed, and on remand found that § 418.361(2)(g) was unconstitutional because it had a statute of limitations of 500 weeks less 30 days for making claims of loss of industrial use. On review, the court found that he did not show that the statute created arbitrary classifications, that the tests for equal protection and due process were whether the statute bore a reasonable relation to a permissible legislative objective, and that the goal of protecting defendant injury fund was legitimately taken into account by the legislature. The court found that § 418.361(2)(g) satisfied due process and equal protection, but remanded the matter to the Board because the employee did not show that he had pain preventing his use of both legs in industry.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and ruled that the statute did not violate due process or equal protection. The court remanded the matter to the Board for further determination of whether the employee had suffered the loss of industrial use of his legs.

Davey v. Detroit Auto. Inter-Insurance Exchange


Docket No. 65227


Supreme Court of Michigan


414 Mich. 1; 322 N.W.2d 541; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 545


October 14, 1981, Argued  
August 9, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 98 Mich App 123; 296 NW2d 12 (1980).
Davey v. Detroit Auto. Inter-Insurance Exchange, 98 Mich. App. 123, 296 N.W.2d 12, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2729 (1980)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant insurer challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) that affirmed a trial court judgment awarding survivor's loss benefits with a cost of living adjustment to plaintiff widow under the insurer's no-fault insurance policy. The intermediate appellate court held that the failure to provide such an increase in Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3108 (1972) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.13108 (1972)) violated equal protection and due process.


OVERVIEW: The widow's husband died in an automobile accident. The trial court and intermediate appellate court agreed with her argument that the legislature violated equal protection and due process when it failed to provide recipients of no-fault survivor's benefits with a cost of living increase but provided such an increase to recipients of work loss benefits in Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3107(b) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.13107(b)). The court reversed the decision and remanded the action to the trial court. The court determined that the proper test for evaluating the legislation's constitutionality as to both due process and equal protection was to determine whether it bore a reasonable relation to a permissible legislative objective. The provision of assured, adequate, and prompt recovery for certain economic losses resulting from automobile accidents was a permissible objective to which the legislation's benefits were reasonably related. The legislature had a rational basis for treating recipients of survivor's benefits differently from recipients of work loss benefits in that survivor's benefits were broader and included items in addition to salary and wages.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court that had affirmed a trial court judgment awarding survivor's loss benefits with a cost of living increase to plaintiff widow under the insurer's no-fault automobile insurance policy. The action was remanded to the trial court for recomputation of the widow's survivor's loss benefits.

Wikman v. Novi


Docket No. 62843


Supreme Court of Michigan


413 Mich. 617; 322 N.W.2d 103; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 543


December 3, 1979, Argued  
July 2, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Wikman v. City of Novi, 406 Mich. 977, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 1425 (1979)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners filed an action in circuit court that sought injunctive relief from a special tax assessment on their property issued by defendants city and treasurer. The circuit court granted the injunction. Pursuant to the Tax Tribunal Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 205.701 et seq.; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 7.650(1) et seq., the Michigan Court of Appeals reversed and remanded to the Tax Tribunal. The property owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The property owners sought injunctive relief from a special assessment used to fund improvements to a road near their real estate. The court held that the Tax Tribunal Act granted the Tax Tribunal exclusive jurisdiction over this proceeding because the special assessments were clearly in the nature of a property tax. The Legislature specifically delegated its power to levy special assessments for public improvements under Mich. Const. art. 4 § 1 (1963) to the Tax Tribunal. Although the circuit courts traditionally exercised jurisdiction over actions to enjoin collection of special assessments, that jurisdiction was divested by the Tax Tribunal Act. Further, it was proper to remand the action to the Tax Tribunal because it was given authority to issue writs, orders, or directives even though it lacked the power to issue an injunction. Finally, the court noted that the time limitations under the Tax Tribunal Act did not prohibit the action because the circuit court action was filed within the applicable limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that reversed and remanded the action to the Tax Tribunal.

Sexton v. Ryder Truck Rental, Inc.


Docket Nos. 61606, 63362


Supreme Court of Michigan


413 Mich. 406; 320 N.W.2d 843; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 523


April 15, 1980, Argued  
June 14, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 84 Mich App 69; 269 NW2d 308 (1978) reversed.

 90 Mich App 612; 282 NW2d 417 (1979).
Sexton v. Ryder Truck Rental, 84 Mich. App. 69, 269 N.W.2d 308, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2465 (1978)
Storie v. Southfield Leasing, 90 Mich. App. 612, 282 N.W.2d 417, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 2197 (1979)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals in Sexton is reversed, and the judgment of the Court of Appeals in Storie is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Michigan residents appealed two decisions of the Michigan Court of Appeals. In both cases, plaintiffs had been injured or killed in out of state accidents. In the first case, the appellate court applied the doctrine of lex loci delicti and ruled against plaintiff on the ground that Michigan law did not apply. In the second case, the appellate court abandoned the doctrine, applied Michigan law, and held that plaintiff could recover.


OVERVIEW: One plaintiff was injured when a truck leased from defendant truck rental company, which did business in Michigan, was involved in an accident in Virginia. Plaintiff sued company under the Michigan motor vehicle owners' liability statute, but the trial court granted summary judgment on the ground that Virginia law applied, and company was not liable under Virginia law. The appellate court affirmed. In the second case, plaintiff was killed when a plane owned by a Michigan corporation crashed in Ohio. Plaintiff decedent's wife sued under the Michigan aircraft owners' liability statute. Again, the trial court granted summary judgment to corporation on the ground that it was not liable under Ohio law, but the appellate court reversed. The court held that the doctrine of lex loci delicti did not apply to cases where all of the parties were either Michigan residents or doing business within the state. The court also held that the Michigan owners' liability statutes could properly be applied because the owner-operator relationship took place exclusively in Michigan, and the statutes were not therefore actually given extraterritorial application.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the first appellate court decision and affirmed the second.

In re Appeal of Apportionment of Wayne County


Docket Nos. 69146, 69155


Supreme Court of Michigan


413 Mich. 224; 321 N.W.2d 615; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 515


April 20, 1982, Argued  
May 5, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Apportionment of Wayne County Bd. of Commissioners-1982, 413 Mich. 858, 1982 Mich. LEXIS 1454 (1982)
Apportionment of Ingham County Bd. of Commissioners-1982, 413 Mich. 858, 1982 Mich. LEXIS 1404 (1982)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, two county boards of commissioners, sought review of judgments of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which declined to approve their apportionment plans and remanded to the apportionment commissions.


OVERVIEW: Both apportionment commissions sought to achieve zero or substantially zero population deviation plans. The invalidity under the federal Constitution of the provision for one representative on the board of supervisors from each township, Mich. Const. art. 7, § 7, posed the question whether the constitutional scheme of township and city representation in county boards was severable. The court held that the legislature could not provide for representation on the board of commissioners other than from townships and cities except to the extent required by the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. The court held that Acts 261 and 293 required that commissioner district lines be drawn to preserve township, village, city and precinct lines to the extent this could be done without exceeding the range of allowable divergence under the federal Constitution, 11.9 percent, at the least cost to the federal principle of equal population between election districts consistent with the maximum preservation of such lines. Between two or more alternative plans, which comply with that standard, compactness and squareness in shape to the extent practicable shall govern.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments but not the reasoning of the trial court.

In re Apportionment of State Legislature-1982


Docket No. 68777


Supreme Court of Michigan


413 Mich. 96; 321 N.W.2d 565; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 514


March 5, 1982, Argued  
March 25, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal dismissed by the Supreme Court of the United States on October 12, 1982.
Appeal dismissed by Kleiner v. Sanderson, 459 U.S. 900, 103 S. Ct. 201, 74 L. Ed. 2d 161, 1982 U.S. LEXIS 3776 (1982)


PRIOR HISTORY: In re Apportionment of State Legislatur, 412 Mich. 1109, 1982 Mich. LEXIS 1822 (1982)



DISPOSITION: The Court denied a motion for rehearing on May 21, 1982.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Various legislative reapportionment plans were submitted to the court by the Commission on Legislative Apportionment, with the court called on to order one such plan adopted.


OVERVIEW: In an original proceeding, the reapportionment of the state legislature was submitted to the court for decision. This submission was made after a bipartisan commission could not agree on a reapportionment plan. The court refused to consider the commission's various plans, and instead ordered that new submissions be made by what was in effect a special master. In so ruling, the court held that the plans submitted by the bipartisan commission could not be considered by it because the commission itself was no longer a viable entity, inasmuch as it was obliged to recommend plans that considered factors other than population. Because deciding legislative district boundaries based upon factors other than population was constitutionally intolerable, the commission's very existence was invalid. Consequently, the court would not consider the several plans submitted to it by the commission, and instead ordered a special master to submit one or more plans to it for approval.


OUTCOME: The court held that the Commission on Legislative Apportionment was not a valid entity, inasmuch as the rules it was required to apply were constitutionally invalid. A special master was therefore appointed and ordered to submit reapportionment plans to the court.

Day v. W. A. Foote Memorial Hospital, Inc.


Docket No. 61821


Supreme Court of Michigan


412 Mich. 698; 316 N.W.2d 712; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 494


January 9, 1980, Argued  
March 2, 1982, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The decision of the WCAB is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant widower sought review of a decision of the Workers' Compensation Appeal Board (WCAB) (Michigan), which denied his claim for benefits based on the death of his wife against appellees, an employer and insurer under Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.331, the Worker's Disability Compensation Act.


OVERVIEW: The court granted the widower leave to appeal whether the conclusive presumption of a widow's dependency on her husband for financial support under the Act was an unconstitutional gender-based discrimination against widowers under the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution. On appeal, the court affirmed the WCAB's denial of benefits. The court held that the presumption of a widow's dependency under the Act was unconstitutional and that all surviving spouses were required to prove their dependency in fact instead of extending the presumption of dependency to all surviving spouses. The United States Supreme Court held unconstitutional another state's workers' compensation law that was similar to the Act and stated that the state courts should determine an appropriate remedy in consideration of the overall legislative purpose of the unconstitutional statute. The court found that requiring proof of dependency by all spouses was consistent with the legislative history of the Act. The court affirmed the decision of the WCAB because the widower stipulated that he had not been dependent on his deceased wife and no purpose would have been served by remand to the WCAB.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the WCAB.

Michigan Dep't of Civil Rights ex rel. Parks v. General Motors Corp., Fisher Body Div.


Docket No. 64141


Supreme Court of Michigan


412 Mich. 610; 317 N.W.2d 16; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 493; 35 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 957; 29 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P32,829


November 14, 1980, Argued  
March 1, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: GMC v. Dep't of Civ. Rights ex rel. Parks, 93 Mich. App. 366, 287 N.W.2d 240, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 2433 (1979)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The appellant Michigan Department of Civil Rights (commission) sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed a judgment of the circuit court reversing a finding by the commission that the appellee corporation had discriminated against the claimant because of her exercise of religion.


OVERVIEW: The appellant Michigan Department of Civil Rights (commission) held in an administrative hearing that the appellee corporation had discriminated against the claimant because of her religion. The commission based its decision on the fact that the claimant was placed on a shift which included hours of her Sabbath, she was subsequently discharged when she failed to report for work on three occasions, and the corporation took no action to accommodate her. The order was reversed by the circuit court, which was affirmed by the court of appeals. On review, the court held that the Fair Employment Practices Act (FEPA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 423.303 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.458(3)), and Mich. Const. art. 5, § 29 (1963), prohibited discrimination against any person because of religion. It was the duty of the commission under the constitution to investigate allegations of discrimination, and to secure the equal protection of civil rights without discrimination. The court held that while the FEPA did not impose a separate obligation to accommodate the religious needs of employees, an employer could be required to act affirmatively to avoid discriminating against an employee because of religion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded judgment of the court of appeals to determine whether there was a factual basis for a finding of discrimination based on evidence that the appellee corporation failed to act affirmatively to avoid the discriminatory effect of a facially neutral practice.

Socialist Workers Party v. Secretary of State


Docket No. 65466


Supreme Court of Michigan


412 Mich. 571; 317 N.W.2d 1; 1982 Mich. LEXIS 495


January 8, 1981, Argued  
March 1, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Socialist Workers Party v. Sec'y of State, 409 Mich. 896, 1980 Mich. LEXIS 919 (1980)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff political party appealed the judgment from the Wayne Circuit Court (Michigan), which granted judgment to defendant state in the political party's action to have amendments to Mich. Comp. Laws § 168.685 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 6.1685) and Mich. Comp. Laws §§ 168.560a and 168.560b (Mich. Stat. Ann. §§ 6.1560(1) and 6.1560(2)) declared unconstitutional. The laws required new political parties to meet petition and primary vote requirements.


OVERVIEW: A political party (party) filed this action for declaratory judgment that a law requiring a new political party to meet a petition requirement and a minimum primary vote requirement was unconstitutional. The trial court granted judgment to the state. The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded. The court held that a prior decision of a federal court did not bar this case under res judicata. That case involved an issue of law, while this case involved an "as applied" constitutionality challenge. The court held that the law imposed both unreasonable and unnecessary restrictions to access to the general election ballot and thus violated the Michigan equal protection clause, Mich. Const. art. 1, § 2. The law unconstitutionally classified new political parties separately from established ones and burdened the party's supporters' fundamental rights of association and to cast their votes effectively. The state failed to establish that this classification was necessary to serve a compelling interest. The law also violated Mich. Const. art. 2, § 4, the "purity of elections" clause. The law violated the Michigan Constitution's goal of equality of treatment for all candidates.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the circuit court granting judgment to the state and declared the statutes requiring a new political party to meet both petition and minimum primary vote requirements unconstitutional. The court remanded the case.

In re Hague

DISPOSITION: Judge Hague is suspended without pay from his judicial duties and administrative responsibilities for 60 days.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Judicial Tenure Commission charged respondent judge with disobedience of valid orders entered by a superior court, refusal to follow the decisions of higher courts, abuse of the contempt power, and improperly excluding attorneys from his courtroom and recommended a suspension from judicial office in a judicial discipline proceeding.

Gusler v. Fairview Tubular Products


Docket No. 63538


Supreme Court of Michigan


412 Mich. 270; 315 N.W.2d 388; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 308


May 6, 1980, Argued  
December 30, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing granted 414 Mich 1102.
On rehearing at, Appeal dismissed by Gusler v. Fairview Tubular Prods., 414 Mich. 1102, 1982 Mich. LEXIS 700 (1982)


PRIOR HISTORY: Jolliff v. American Advertising Distributors, Inc., 49 Mich App 1; 211 NW2d 160 (1973), overruled.

-- 92 Mich App 164; 284 NW2d 487 (1979).
Gusler v. Fairview Tublar Products, 92 Mich. App. 164, 284 N.W.2d 487, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 2327 (1979)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the employer and the insurer leave to appeal to consider whether the cost of living increases in workers' disability compensation benefits provided for in Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.355 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(355)) applied to the minimum as well as the maximum weekly rates for total disability as established in Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.351 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(351). The employee was appellee.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the heart of the dispute was whether the legislature ever intended that the minimum benefit rates of § 351 were to be adjusted in accordance with § 355. The court found that it did not. The employee claimed, and the workers' compensation board and the court of appeals agreed, that the language following the minimum rates established in § 351 and subjecting them to § 355 should be interpreted as requiring an annual adjustment to minimum rates equal to any adjustment in the maximum rates. The court disagreed with that interpretation and held that no adjustment to the minimum rates prescribed in § 351 was authorized because none was intended by the legislature. The court reached the conclusion because of 1) the legislature's failure to make specific provision for adjustment of minimum rates while explicitly doing so with respect to maximum rates, 2) an analysis of the history of the provision in question, and 3) the internal conflicts and plainly absurd results which plaintiff's construction of the statute would effect. In so holding, the court overruled prior case law in support of the employee's position.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment for the employee and the cause was remanded for recomputation of benefits in conformance with the opinion.

People v. Ingram


Docket No. 66159


Supreme Court of Michigan


412 Mich. 200; 312 N.W.2d 652; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 298



November 23, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 99 Mich App 410; 297 NW2d 684 (1980).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the case is remanded to the Recorder's Court of Detroit for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which granted defendant's motion to suppress and dismissed the offense of carrying a concealed weapon pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.227.


OVERVIEW: The police officers were in the midst of writing out a traffic citation for an illegally parked car when defendant appeared and started to enter the car. The police requested that defendant show them his driver's license and noticed a large bulge in his waist area as they observed him reach for the license. Defendant admitted he had a gun in his waist area in response to the officer's inquiry and was arrested on a charge of carrying a concealed weapon pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.227. Defendant filed an immediate motion to suppress, which was granted on the ground that there was no justification for the officer's request to see defendant's driver's license. The trial court dismissed the case and the appellate court affirmed. The court ruled that the conduct of the police officers in the matter was constitutionally permissible because once they ascertained that defendant was the owner of the vehicle, they properly requested his driver's license in order to issue him the parking citation. Defendant complied with the request, and from that point on the officers noticed the bulge in his waist, inquired whether he had a gun, and upon his admission they seized the weapon.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in favor of the State and overturned the appellate court's and the trial court's dismissal of the State's prosecution against defendant for the offense of carrying a concealed weapon.

People v. Pulley


Docket No. 62820


Supreme Court of Michigan


411 Mich. 523; 309 N.W.2d 170; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 277


May 6, 1980, Argued  
August 24, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Pulley, 407 Mich. 946, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 1612 (1979)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with one count of unlawful delivery of heroin and one count of conspiracy to deliver heroin. He pleaded guilty of attempted possession with intent to deliver heroin and was sentenced to serve 6 to 10 years imprisonment. Defendant appealed from the Court of Appeals' (Michigan) affirmance of his sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was not present at an in-chambers, off-the-record presentence conference between the sentencing judge and defendant's attorney. Defendant asserted that his opportunity for allocution and to refute the contents of the presentence report was made ineffective by his absence from the presentence conference, and that the presentence conference was a critical stage of a criminal prosecution at which a defendant has a constitutional right to be present. Although the court held that defendant had a right to be personally present at every stage of the trial where his substantial rights might have been affected, he was not prejudiced by his absence from the sentence conference. There was no showing that what transpired at the conference was objectionable in any fashion. Thus, the appellate court did not err when it affirmed held that defendant's rights were not violated by his absence from the presentence conference.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

 

237 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Clabin


Docket No. 65138


Supreme Court of Michigan


411 Mich. 472; 307 N.W.2d 682; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 270



July 13, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied post, 411 Mich 1157.
Rehearing denied by People v. Clabin, 411 Mich. 1157, 1981 Mich. LEXIS 1750 (1981)


PRIOR HISTORY: 97 Mich App 340; 294 NW2d 266 (1980).
People v. Clabin, 97 Mich. App. 340, 294 N.W.2d 266, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2660 (1980)



DISPOSITION: The defendant's conviction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, after having pled guilty in the trial court (Michigan) to a charge of possessing a weapon while an inmate contrary to Mich. Comp. Laws § 800.283 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1623), argued that his conviction was invalid because the language of the statute had never been properly re-enacted after it was declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to possessing a weapon while an inmate contrary to Mich. Comp. Laws § 800.283 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1623), then argued that his conviction was invalid because the language of the statute had never been properly re-enacted after it had been declared unconstitutional. The court reversed the conviction, holding that, because the legislature did not, in 1977 Mich. Pub. Acts 164, re-enact the 1972 amendment, Mich. Const. art. 4, § 25 (1963) mandated the conclusion that the legislature's attempt to have remedied the situation was ineffectual. Amendment of the title of an act, after a declaration of unconstitutionality of a portion of the act itself, no matter what the reason for the finding of unconstitutionality, did not suffice to resurrect the voided portion. If the voided portion was to have been again considered a part of the law, then the law had to have been revised, altered, or amended. Section 25 clearly provided that reference to the title only of a law was insufficient. The section of the act which was altered or amended had to have been re-enacted and published at length.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for possessing a weapon while an inmate.

People v. White


Docket No. 62677


Supreme Court of Michigan


411 Mich. 366; 308 N.W.2d 128; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 265


December 3, 1979, Argued  
July 13, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. White, 406 Mich. 975, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 1469 (1979)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the affirmance of his conviction from the Court of Appeals (Michigan) for attempted delivery of heroin pursuant to Mich. Comp. Law § 335.341(1)(a) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 18.1070(41)(1)(a)). Defendant had previously been convicted of both perjury and attempted delivery of heroin, and on appeal, the court of appeals had set aside his perjury conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with attempted delivery of heroin under Mich. Comp. Law § 335.341(1)(a) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 18.1070(41)(1)(a)), and Mich. Comp. Law § 750.92 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.287), after a drug buy with an undercover officer. At the preliminary hearing, defendant denied all involvement in the buy. Defendant was thereafter charged with perjury under Mich. Comp. Law § 750.422 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.664). Defendant pleaded guilty to both counts. Defendant appealed his conviction, and the court of appeals reversed the perjury conviction, but affirmed the attempted delivery of heroin conviction. On appeal, the court reversed defendant's conviction for attempted delivery of heroin. The court found that because the officer approached the defendant, asked him to make the purchase, approached him again and then drove the defendant to make the purchase of narcotics, defendant was entrapped as a matter of law. The court reinstated defendant's perjury conviction, finding that defendant lied about adjudicative facts as well as ultimate facts; thus, there was a sufficient basis for the perjury charge. The court also found a sufficient factual basis for defendant's guilty plea.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for attempted delivery of heroin and reinstated defendant's conviction for perjury.

People v. Barker


Docket Nos. 63359, 63503


Supreme Court of Michigan


411 Mich. 291; 307 N.W.2d 61; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 259


May 6, 1980, Argued  
June 19, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied as to Lynn post, 411 Mich 1157.
Rehearing denied by People v. Lynn, 411 Mich. 1157, 1981 Mich. LEXIS 1748 (1981)


PRIOR HISTORY: 90 Mich App 151; 282 NW2d 266 (1979).

 91 Mich App 117; 283 NW2d 664 (1979).
People v. Slayton, 90 Mich. App. 151, 282 N.W.2d 266, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 2144 (1979)
People v. Lynn, 91 Mich. App. 117, 283 N.W.2d 664, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 2234 (1979)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged decisions from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed judgments from a trial court in kidnapping and first-degree criminal sexual conduct cases. The lower appellate court held that the trial court erred in instructing that the asportation element of kidnapping was satisfied if the jury found movement of the victim to be incidental to the commission of the underlying offense of first-degree criminal sexual conduct.


OVERVIEW: In the first of two cases the complainant charged that defendants forced her into their automobile, knocked her unconscious, drove some 15 to 18 miles into the country, and raped her. Defendants were charged with kidnapping and first-degree criminal sexual conduct. The lower appellate court reversed their kidnapping convictions. In the second of two cases the complainant, a 10-year-old testified that defendant entered her apartment under the pretext of using the phone, robbed the apartment, carried the complainant to a nearby apartment, forced her to engage in fellatio, and unsuccessfully attempted to engage in sexual intercourse with her. Defendant was convicted of kidnapping and first-degree criminal sexual conduct. The lower appellate court reversed defendant's conviction for kidnapping. On appeal, the court held: (1) the asportation element required for kidnapping could not be incidental to another offense when the punishment for that offense was equal to that for kidnapping; and (2) the jury instructions given in the instant cases erroneously allowed the juries to infer the required asportation from movement incidental to the commission of criminal sexual conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decisions from the lower court of appeals, which reversed defendants' convictions for kidnapping.

Oakland County Prosecuting Atty. v. Mich. Dep't of Corr.


Docket No. 67134


Supreme Court of Michigan


411 Mich. 183; 305 N.W.2d 515; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 255


May 6, 1981, Argued  
May 14, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Oakland County v. Dep't of Corr., 411 Mich. 889, 1981 Mich. LEXIS 1275 (1981)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the Michigan Department of Corrections (Department), appealed a decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which entered judgment for plaintiff, the Oakland County prosecutor, in an action for declaratory and injunctive relief. The court of appeals found that the Prison Overcrowding Emergency Powers Act, 1980 Mich. Pub. Acts 519, violated Mich. Const. art. V, § 14 (1963) and enjoined the Department from implementing it.


OVERVIEW: When the Department asked the governor to declare a state of emergency and to implement the Prison Overcrowding Emergency Powers Act, 1980 Mich. Pub. Acts 519, the county prosecutor sought declaratory and injunctive relief. The circuit court entered judgment for the Department; but the court of appeals found that the statute was unconstitutional and reversed. On appeal, the court set forth the legislative history of the Act and described how the Act relieved prison overcrowding by systematically reducing certain prisoners' sentences. Mich. Const. art. V, § 14 gave the governor the power to commute sentences. The court offered cases to illustrate that the judiciary did not interfere with the governor's power. But the court explained that, like the executive and judicial branches, the legislature had a role to play in establishing the length of sentences too. Mich. Const. art. IV, § 45 increased the legislature's power to issue indeterminate sentences and authorized it to enact the Prison Overcrowding Emergency Powers Act. Whether it was wise for all three branches effectively to share the power to commute sentences was a political question, not a constitutional one.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' decision and entered judgment for the department.

Falk v. State Bar of Mich.


Docket No. 60722


Supreme Court of Michigan


411 Mich. 63; 305 N.W.2d 201; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 263


June 5, 1979, Argued  
April 29, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied post, 1157, without prejudice to plaintiff's presenting the issues raised in further proceedings in this pending case.
Rehearing denied by, Without prejudice Falk v. State Bar of Mich., 411 Mich. 1157, 1981 Mich. LEXIS 1742 (1981)
Supplemental opinion at, Petition dismissed by Falk v. State Bar of Michigan, 418 Mich. 270, 342 N.W.2d 504, 1983 Mich. LEXIS 269 (1983)


PRIOR HISTORY: Falk v. State Bar of Mich., 402 Mich. 960, 1978 Mich. LEXIS 1101 (1978)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lawyer brought a petition for special relief objecting to a number of activities conducted by defendant bar association.


OVERVIEW: The lawyer brought a petition for special relief challenging activities of the bar association, asserting his First Amendment objections to the activities and expenditures of the bar association to which he was required to pay dues. The court ruled that a significant impairment of First Amendment rights must survive exacting scrutiny and that this type of scrutiny was necessary even if any deterrent effect on the exercise of First Amendment rights arose, not through direct government action, but indirectly as an unintended but inevitable result of the government's conduct. Further, the court held that encroachment could not be justified upon a mere showing of a legitimate state interest, but that the interest advanced must be paramount and that the burden was on the government to show the existence of such an interest. The court denied the petition in all respects but one, concluding that the lawyer's name must be removed from a roster of the bar association sold for commercial use, finding that the record failed to demonstrate a compelling state interest which could not be served by means less intrusive on the First Amendment rights of an objecting individual attorney.


OUTCOME: A petition for special relief was denied in all respects but one, and the court ordered that the lawyer's name be removed from a roster of the bar association sold for commercial use.
