Poletown Neighborhood Council v. Detroit


Docket No. 66294


Supreme Court of Michigan


410 Mich. 616; 304 N.W.2d 455; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 250; 19 ERC (BNA) 1972; 11 ELR 20778


March 3, 1981, Argued  
March 13, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Dissenting Opinion of Justice Ryan Filed April 20, 1981.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a neighborhood association, property owners, and residents, sought review of the decision from a trial court (Michigan), which granted judgment in favor of defendant city in an action by plaintiffs to prevent the city form taking land by condemnation under the Economic Development Corporations Act and giving the land to a private entity to promote industry and commerce.


OVERVIEW: The city planned to acquire land held by private owners under the authority of the Act in order to convey the land to a private company for the creation of an industrial site. Plaintiffs filed an action to challenge the project. The trial court found that the city did not abuse its discretion in determining that the property was necessary for the project and dismissed the complaint. Plaintiffs challenged the decision, and the court granted the motion for immediate consideration and an application for bypass. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that under Mich. Comp. Laws § 125.1622 the legislature authorized cities to acquire property by condemnation in order to provide industrial and commercial sites and the means of transfer from the cities to private users. The project was developed to promote the public health and welfare, which was authorized by Mich. Const. art 4, § 51 (1963). The primary focus of the project was the creation of jobs and the promotion of the public welfare, and the benefit created for the private corporation was incidental. Finally, the Michigan Environmental Protection Act did not apply where there was adverse impact on social and environment cultures.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the city and the dismissal of plaintiffs' complaint challenging the propriety of the city's condemnation proceedings.

People v. Lynch


Docket No. 63061


Supreme Court of Michigan


410 Mich. 343; 301 N.W.2d 796; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 237


April 15, 1980, Argued  
February 23, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Lynch, 407 Mich. 897, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 1196 (1979)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgment of the Court of Appeals is reversed and Lynch's conviction is reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed defendant's conviction for possession of a gas-ejecting device.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.224 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.421) (the statute) of possessing a gas-ejecting device. The appellate court vacated his conviction, holding that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad because it failed to sufficiently distinguish between legal and illegal gas-ejecting devices and did not provide sufficient notice as to what conduct was prohibited. The court reversed the appellate court's decision. The statute clearly reached gas-ejecting weapons such as defendant attempted to carry and, thus, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to him. Vagueness challenges that did not involve First Amendment freedoms were to be examined in the light of the facts of the case, and one to whom application of a statute was constitutional would not be heard to attack the statute on the ground that impliedly it might also be taken as applying to other persons or other situations in which its application might be unconstitutional. The statute clearly prohibited the possession of weapons and the meaning of the phrase "gas-ejecting device" could be ascertained from the accompanying words.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision, which reversed the trial court's decision convicting defendant of possession of a gas-ejecting device.

Robinson Township v. Knoll


Docket No. 58747


Supreme Court of Michigan


410 Mich. 293; 302 N.W.2d 146; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 236; 17 A.L.R.4th 79


January 8, 1980, Argued  
February 23, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wyoming Twp v Herweyer, 321 Mich 611; 33 NW2d 93 (1948).

 70 Mich App 258; 245 NW2d 709 (1976).
Robinson Township v. Knoll, 70 Mich. App. 258, 245 N.W.2d 709, 1976 Mich. App. LEXIS 840 (1976)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals is vacated and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff township filed an action against defendant landowners seeking the removal of a mobile home from the landowners' parcel of land. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) held that the township ordinance segregating the mobile homes from residential homes was constitutional. The landowners filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: The landowners had an 80-acre parcel of land on which they placed a mobile home. The township alleged that the use of the mobile home was contrary to a township's zoning ordinance, which provided that mobile homes could only be located in mobile-home parks, and an ordinance which required that a building permit be obtained before the erection of a building or structure on any property in the township. The landowners contended that the zoning ordinance arbitrarily and capriciously prohibited a proper land use, was overbroad, and failed to establish clear standards to be observed by property owners and citizens of the township. The court vacated the court of appeal's judgment and held that the ordinance was per se unconstitutional. The court found that: (1) the per se exclusion of mobile homes from all areas not designated as mobile-home parks had no reasonable basis under the police power, (2) a building permit could not have been issued because of the per se rule confining mobile homes to mobile-home parks, and (3) there was no reasonable basis for distinguishing between mobile homes and other prefabricated dwellings which were allowed in residential areas.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the court of appeals, which upheld as constitutional a township's ordinance prohibiting mobile homes in a residential area. The court remanded the case to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Krueger v. Williams


Docket Nos. 59743, 61861


Supreme Court of Michigan


410 Mich. 144; 300 N.W.2d 910; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 233; 17 A.L.R.4th 902


January 8, 1980, Argued  
February 3, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal Dismissed by the Supreme Court of the United States June 22, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: 75 Mich App 1; 245 NW2d 615 (1977).



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the Court of Appeals are reversed and the cases are remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, the court granted leave to appeal judgments of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) that denied a motion filed pursuant to GCR 105.8 (1963) to allow substituted service upon defendant by service of process on the liability carrier and in the second case granted a motion for substituted service by publication to obtain personal jurisdiction over defendant.


OVERVIEW: In two separate actions involving car accidents, the court granted leave to appeal decisions regarding motions to allow substituted service filed pursuant to Mich. Ct. R., GCR 105.8 (1963). In the first case, the lower court denied plaintiffs' motion to allow substituted service upon an absent defendant by service of process on the liability carrier. In the second case, the lower court granted the motion to allow substituted service by publication. The court held that in the first case, the lower court erred by not allowing some form of substituted service of process, where plaintiff made diligent efforts to locate defendant, an investigator was hired and various locations checked, and the insurance company was aware of the possibility that a suit would be filed. In the second case, the court held that the verified motion for substituted service was insufficient because it failed to show with particularity either that service of process could not reasonably have been made according to prescribed methods or that no better alternative means of giving notice were available other than publication. The court reversed the judgment in the first case and remanded both cases.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in the first case that denied a motion to allow substituted service upon defendant by service of process on the liability carrier and remanded for further proceedings. In the second case, the court held that the pleadings contained insufficient factual assertions as to what diligent attempts were taken to find and serve defendants by means other than publication and remanded for plaintiffs to show due diligence.

Leblanc v. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co.


Docket No. 62439


Supreme Court of Michigan


410 Mich. 173; 301 N.W.2d 775; 1981 Mich. LEXIS 234


January 9, 1980, Argued  
February 3, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Leblnanc v. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co., 411 Mich. 1119, 306 N.W.2d 311, 1981 Mich. LEXIS 1741 (1981)


PRIOR HISTORY: Le Blanc v. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co., 87 Mich. App. 555, 274 N.W.2d 69, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2709 (1978)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insured filed suit to recover from defendant insurer no-fault benefits for Medicare-reimbursed hospital and medical expenses. The appellate court (Michigan) held that Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3109 subd. (1) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.13109 subd. (1)) of the Michigan no-fault insurance act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3101 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.13101 et seq.), required a set-off of Medicare payments. The insured sought review.


OVERVIEW: The insured, over the age of 65 and eligible for Medicare benefits, sustained multiple injuries in an accident. The insurer paid no-fault benefits with respect to qualified items of hospital and medical care not covered by Medicare, but denied compensation for those expenses which were picked up by Medicare, claiming that Medicare benefits were required to be subtracted from no-fault benefits under the no-fault act (act). The court found that such payments came under the set-off option of Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3109a (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.13109(1)) of the act as other health and accident coverage. The court reasoned that the phrase "other health and accident coverage" contained in that section contemplated benefits provided to qualified participants under the Medicare program. Thus, Medicare benefits could be coordinated with no-fault personal protection insurance benefits at the option of the insured. Because the insured did not elect to coordinate his Medicare benefits with his no-fault benefits, payments made on his behalf by the Medicare program should not have been subtracted from the no-fault benefits due under the no-fault policy issued to him by the insurer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court and held that payments made on the insured's behalf by the Medicare program could not be subtracted from the no-fault benefits due under the no-fault policy issued to him by the insurer.

 83 Mich App 467; 268 NW2d 881 (1978).



DISPOSITION: The act is not unconstitutional as applied in these cases. The decision of the Court of Appeals in Van Slooten is affirmed, that in Bickel is reversed, and the cases are remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, defendants, surface estate owners, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan). Plaintiff owners of surface estates filed suit to quiet oil and gas interests that were created prior to the passage of the act. Defendants contended that the application of the dormant mineral act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 554.291 et seq. was unconstitutional.

Laws § 554.291 et seq., plaintiff landowners sold the land by a deed excepting from the conveyance title to the oil and mineral estates. Pursuant to the act, the interests were deemed abandoned and vested in the owners of the surface estates because the owners of the severed oil and gas interests had not performed any of the acts specified or recorded a claim of interest for a period of over 20 years. The court noted that the act exempted from the abandonment provisions certain interests. The court held that the act did not unconstitutionally change defendants' property rights into mere causes of action. The court affirmed one case, reversed the other, and remanded for further proceedings.

The court reversed the lower court's decision that held that the act unconstitutionally impaired the obligation of a contract. O'Brien v. Hazelet & Erdal


Docket Nos. 61666, 61342, 61483, 61498, 61499, 61500, 61501


Supreme Court of Michigan


410 Mich. 1; 299 N.W.2d 336; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 258


April 3, 1979, Argued  
December 23, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 84 Mich App 764; 270 NW2d 690 (1978).

 82 Mich App 368; 266 NW2d 850 (1978).

 83 Mich App 167; 268 NW2d 332 (1978).

 82 Mich App 291; 266 NW2d 795 (1978).
O'Brien v. Hazelet & Erdal, 84 Mich. App. 764, 270 N.W.2d 690, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2547 (1978)
Bouser v. Lincoln Park, 83 Mich. App. 167, 268 N.W.2d 332, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2289 (1978)
Oole v. Oosting, 82 Mich. App. 291, 266 N.W.2d 795, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2224 (1978)
Muzar v. Metro Town Houses, 82 Mich. App. 368, 266 N.W.2d 850, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2231 (1978)



DISPOSITION: The judgments of the Court of Appeals are affirmed as to O'Brien v Hazelet & Erdal, Bouser v Lincoln Park and Oole v Oosting, and reversed as to Muzar v Metro Town Houses, Inc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In four separate cases, plaintiff injured parties alleged damages arising out of defective real estate improvements for which defendants, state-licensed architects and engineers, had furnished the construction design. The Michigan Court of Appeals declared the six-year statute of limitations, Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.5839(1), valid in one case and invalid in three cases. The court consolidated the cases on appeal.


OVERVIEW: The injured parties argued that the statute violated due process because it barred a cause of action before the necessary elements constituting the cause of action were present. The injured parties also asserted that the statute did not afford potential plaintiffs a reasonable time within which to bring suit. The court concluded that the statute was both one of limitation and one of repose. Therefore, the court determined that the injured parties were not deprived of the right to sue a state-licensed architect or engineer because no such right could have arisen after the statutory period elapsed. The court also rejected the argument that the statute violated equal protection by singling out the victims of the negligence of architects and engineers for a burden not imposed upon the victims of other tortfeasors and by failing to extend the same protection to others, such as contractors, who might have been responsible for defects in real estate improvements. The court concluded that the legislature could have rationally determined that state-licensed architects and engineers possessed characteristics that reasonably distinguished them with respect to the object of the legislation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the three judgments that found that statute valid. The court reversed the judgment that found the statute invalid. The court remanded all four cases to the trial courts for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

Great American Ins. Co. v. Queen


Docket No. 62185


Supreme Court of Michigan


410 Mich. 73; 300 N.W.2d 895; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 261


October 2, 1979, Argued  
December 23, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 86 Mich App 362; 272 NW2d 659 (1978).
Great American Ins. Co. v. Queen, 86 Mich. App. 362, 272 N.W.2d 659, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2597 (1978)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, an employer's insurer brought suit for reimbursement of the worker's compensation through the no-fault award. The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed the trial court's order granting summary judgment to the employee. The insurer appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant employee was injured in a car accident while on the job. He collected worker's compensation from the insurer and no-fault insurance from third-party defendant tortfeasors. The question, certified on appeal, was whether the insurer was entitled to reimbursement of economic loss benefits out of the employee's no-fault recovery. Although the court recognized an insurer was due reimbursement if the no-fault award covered the same damages as the worker's compensation award, the right to reimbursement for economic loss did not permit the insurer to reduce a no-fault award for noneconomic loss. The court found that the no-fault law, with its corresponding personal injury protection (PIP), Mich. Comp. Laws §500.3135 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.13135), which limited reimbursement from tort recovery intended to make the injured party whole, was repugnant to the older worker's compensation law, Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.827(5) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(827(5)), which required the injured parity to reimburse the insurer out of any other tort recovery. Through repeal by implication, the court ruled that § 418.827(5) was inapplicable to any recovery obtained under the no-fault law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of the employee and dismissing the insurer's suit for reimbursement of a worker's compensation payment through a no-fault award.

 

250 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Aaron


Docket Nos. 57376, 61140, 61194


Supreme Court of Michigan


409 Mich. 672; 299 N.W.2d 304; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 254; 13 A.L.R.4th 1180


March 6, 1979, Argued  
November 24, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied as to Thompson December 23, 1980.
Appeal after remand at People v. Thompson, 424 Mich. 118, 379 N.W.2d 49, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 1011 (1985)


PRIOR HISTORY: 63 Mich App 230; 234 NW2d 462 (1975).

 81 Mich App 348; 265 NW2d 632 (1978).

 80 Mich App 172; 262 NW2d 917 (1977).
People v. Wright, 80 Mich. App. 172, 262 N.W.2d 917, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1265 (1977)
People v. Thompson, 81 Mich. App. 348, 265 N.W.2d 632, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2137 (1978)



DISPOSITION: In People v Aaron, the judgment of conviction of second-degree murder is reversed, and the case is remanded to the trial court for a new trial. In People v Thompson and People v Wright, the decisions of the Court of Appeals are affirmed, and the cases are remanded to the trial court for new trials.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In the first of three cases consolidated for review, defendant appealed from the Michigan Court of Appeals' affirmation of a conviction of first-degree murder for a homicide committed during an armed robbery. In the second and third cases, the prosecution appealed the reversal of defendant's murder convictions by the Michigan Court of Appeals because of the lack of a malice jury instruction.


OVERVIEW: The Michigan Supreme Court consolidated three cases for determinations whether Michigan had a felony-murder rule that allowed the element of malice required for murder be satisfied by intent to commit the underlying felony or whether the trier of fact must otherwise find malice. The court held Michigan did not have a statutory felony-murder rule, and the court abolished the common-law doctrine of felony-murder. The court stated that such a rule was unnecessary and in many cases unjust in that it violated the basic premise of individual moral culpability upon which criminal law was based. The court further held that in order to convict defendant of murder it should have been shown that he acted with the intent to kill or to inflict great bodily harm or with wanton and willful disregard of the likelihood that he would cause such harm. Lastly, the court held that the issue of malice must always be submitted to a jury.


OUTCOME: The first conviction of murder was reversed, and the second and third case reversals were affirmed. The court held that Michigan did not have a statutory felony-murder rule, and the court abolished the common-law doctrine of felony-murder.

Woll v. Attorney General; People v. Posner; People v. Keane


Docket Nos. 61257, 60809, 60810


Supreme Court of Michigan


409 Mich. 500; 297 N.W.2d 578; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 249


April 3, 1979, Argued  
October 3, 1980, Decided  
October 3, 1980, Opinions in Both Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Order on motion for rehearing post, 551.


PRIOR HISTORY: 80 Mich App 721; 265 NW2d 23 (1978) affirmed.

 79 Mich App 63; 261 NW2d 209 (1977) reversed.
Woll v. Kelley, 80 Mich. App. 721, 265 N.W.2d 23, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2090 (1978)
People v. Posner, 79 Mich. App. 63, 261 N.W.2d 209, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 837 (1977)



DISPOSITION: The indictments against Posner and Keane are dismissed; Woll v Attorney General is remanded to the Court of Appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant lawyers were indicted for conspiring to solicit personal injury claims, a violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.410(1) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.642(1)). Plaintiff lawyer, later indicted, sought declaratory and injunctive relief against the statute. After trial court rulings, the Court of Appeals (Michigan) held the statute unconstitutional in one case, constitutional in the other. Plaintiff lawyer and the state appealed.


OVERVIEW: The lawyers contended that the statute was void for vagueness and deprived them of equal protection. One lawyer contended that the statute was inapplicable to workers' compensation cases. The court held that the legislature intended that the statute apply to workers' compensation cases where the claim arose from an accident but, because the statute lacked requisite notice, the court's construction had only prospective application. The limitation to prosecution of personal injury claims was rationally related to state interests and did not deny equal protection; the legislature could have concluded that solicitation of personal injury claims presented a risk of harm significantly different from solicitation of other legal business. The statute was unconstitutionally overbroad but the overbreadth could be cured by a limiting construction and the statute then applied retroactively. Because the grand juries' assessment of the evidence was not circumscribed by a limiting construction, the indictments had to be dismissed without prejudice to reindictment after a limiting construction was placed on the statute.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed without prejudice the indictments against defendant lawyers. The court remanded the action brought by plaintiff lawyer to the Court of Appeals to fashion a limiting construction on the statute.

Ferency v. Secretary of State


Docket Nos. 65764, 65765, 65882


Supreme Court of Michigan


409 Mich. 569; 297 N.W.2d 544; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 252


September 12, 1980, Argued; September 30, 1980, Submitted on Briefs  
September 12, 1980; October 3, 1980, Decided  
October 3, 1980, Opinions in Both Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Ferency v. Sec'y of State, 409 Mich. 912, 1980 Mich. LEXIS 1128 (1980)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Ingham Circuit Court (Michigan) ruled that ballot proposal petitions did not comply with Mich. Comp. Law § 168.482, Mich. Stat. Ann. § 6.1482, requiring that the petition state all existing state constitutional provisions the proposal would have altered or abridged. The trial court ordered the proposal not placed on ballot. Defendants, Secretary of State, and Board of State Canvassers (board) and intervenor petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the trial court's interpretation of § 168.482 was very complicated and too onerous a burden upon the right of popular amendment. Mich. Const. art. 12, § 2 only required such citation by the petition if the proposed amendment would have directly altered or abridged a specific existing provision. An existing provision was altered or abridged only if the proposal would have rendered it inoperative, added to it, deleted it, or changed its wording. The petition did cite the directly affected provisions. The issue was not rendered moot by the board's failure to certify the petition within 60 days of the election, Mich. Const. art. 12, § 2, Mich. Comp. Law § 168.477, Mich. Stat. Ann. § 6.1477. The certification deadline only passed because the trial court prohibited the board from taking up the issue. The petitioners did not allow the court proceedings to halt their efforts to meet the constitution's petition requirements, as exemplified by the emergency appeal to the court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, and ordered that the Secretary of State and the board attend to its statutory duties related the petition.

People v. Anderson

The state contended that the trial court erred in dismissing the greater charges over its objection and before it rested its case.


OVERVIEW: After defendant testified that the shooting was to prevent her rape, the trial court accepted her plea of guilty to the lesser included offense of manslaughter, over the state's objection and before the state rested its case, and dismissed the state's charges of murder in the first and second degree. Metropolitan Council 23, etc. v. Oakland County


Docket No. 63127


Supreme Court of Michigan


409 Mich. 299; 294 N.W.2d 578; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 240; 105 L.R.R.M. 3424


January 10, 1980, Argued  
July 16, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 89 Mich App 564; 280 NW2d 600 (1979).
Metropolitan Council 23, etc. v. Oakland County (Prosecutor's Investigators), 89 Mich. App. 564, 280 N.W.2d 600, 1979 Mich. App. LEXIS 2102 (1979)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Complainant investigators sought to initiate compulsory, binding public police and fire department interest arbitration proceedings pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 423.231 et seq., (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.455(31)) (Act). The appellate court (Michigan) ruled in the investigators' favor.


OVERVIEW: In reversing the appellate court's decision, the court upheld the administrative factual finding that the investigators were subject to the hazards of county department employees engaged as police officers as supported by competent, material, and substantial evidence on the whole record, but held that the administrative tribunal and the appellate court erred as a matter of law in ruling that the investigators were permitted to invoke the Act as having fulfilled the sole statutory precondition that the employees be either police officers or subject to the hazards of police officers. Guided by time-honored principles of statutory construction, and construing the Act as a whole so as to effectuate its manifest intent, permitting the investigators to invoke the Act was contrary to the legislative intent and arguably led to absurd and unjust results for which there was an otherwise acceptable and supplementary remedy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court, which allowed the investigators to initiate arbitration proceedings.

Gose v. Monroe Auto Equipment Co.


Docket Nos. 60752, 60759, 60826, 60839


Supreme Court of Michigan


409 Mich. 147; 294 N.W.2d 165; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 234


December 5, 1978, Argued  
June 27, 1980, Decided

DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed in each case.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, workers' compensation claimants in two separate actions, sought review of judgments from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), reversing in each case the decisions of the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board that found the claimants workers' compensation claims were barred by the doctrine of res judicata.


OVERVIEW: The two claimants, working for separate employers, were each injured while at work and obtained workers' compensation benefits in separate cases. Subsequently, the claimants sought additional benefits for their injuries. The Workers' Compensation Appeals Board found that both claims were barred by the doctrine of res judicata. The court of appeals reversed the board's decisions. The court held that because both subsequent claims were for additional damages arising out of the original injury, the claims were therefore barred by res judicata. The court overturned the judgments from the court of appeals. The court held that the doctrine of res judicata applied not only to facts previously litigated, but it was also conclusive of all matters that could have been adjudicated at that time.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments in each case for entry of an order denying benefits based on res judicata.

People v. McDonald


Docket No. 62077


Supreme Court of Michigan


409 Mich. 110; 293 N.W.2d 588; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 232


November 13, 1979, Argued  
June 24, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Application denied by People v. McDonald, 442 Mich. 918, 442 Mich. 920, 503 N.W.2d 453, 1993 Mich. LEXIS 1130 (1993)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 86 Mich App 5; 272 NW2d 179 (1978).
People v. McDonald, 86 Mich. App. 5, 272 N.W.2d 179, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2552 (1978)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the appellate court (Michigan), which affirmed the judgment of the trial court, in which defendant was prosecuted for murdering the victim during the perpetration or attempted perpetration of a rape and found guilty but mentally ill under Mich. Comp. Laws §§ 768.36, 750.316 (Mich. Stat. Ann. §§ 28.1059, 28.548) and sentenced to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was prosecuted for murdering the victim during the perpetration or attempted perpetration of a rape and found guilty but mentally ill under Mich. Comp. Laws §§ 768.36, 750.316 (Mich. Stat. Ann. §§ 28.1059, 28.548) and sentenced to life imprisonment. The appellate court affirmed, and on review, the court affirmed because (1) there was no statutory charging error because while the former statute punishing rape was repealed and replaced, the legislature did not intend to abrogate rape as an aggravating circumstance for first-degree murder, and the first-degree murder statute and its included designation of rape were in no way altered; (2) defendant was not denied equal protection as a male by being prosecuted for rape because the classification involved rested upon differences which had a substantial relation to the important governmental object advanced by the legislation; (3) there was no reversible error in the trial court's instructions; and (4) the evidence could reasonably support a finding of guilt beyond a reasonable doubt because, any rational trier of fact could have found the essential elements of the crime beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court (Michigan), which affirmed the judgment of the trial court, in which defendant was prosecuted for murdering the victim during the perpetration or attempted perpetration of a rape and found guilty but mentally ill and sentenced to life imprisonment.

 80 Mich App 125; 263 NW2d 311 (1977).

 80 Mich App 439; 264 NW2d 19 (1978).

 80 Mich App 640; 264 NW2d 83 (1978).
Hickman v. Community Service Ins. Co., 78 Mich. App. 1, 259 N.W.2d 367, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1160 (1977)
Bradley v. Mid Century Ins. Co. (Farmers), 78 Mich. App. 67, 259 N.W.2d 378, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1166 (1977)
American Fidelity Fire Ins. Co. v. Williams, 80 Mich. App. 125, 263 N.W.2d 311, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1260 (1977)
Schigur v. West Bend Mut. Ins. Co., 80 Mich. App. 640, 264 N.W.2d 83, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2081 (1978)
Ellis v. State Farm Ins. Co., 78 Mich. App. 189, 259 N.W.2d 421, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1181 (1977)
Fletcher v. Aetna Casualty & Surety Co., 80 Mich. App. 439, 264 N.W.2d 19, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2056 (1978)



DISPOSITION: The judgments of the Court of Appeals are affirmed as to Bradley, Hickman, Ellis, Ruesing, Davidson, and Fletcher, and are reversed as to Williams and Schigur.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Consolidated appeals were filed against decisions of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which found that exclusions on uninsured motorist coverage prior to the repeal of Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3010 were invalid and allowed a set-off by defendant insurers of no-fault benefits against uninsured motorist coverage after the repeal of § 500.3010.


OVERVIEW: Various plaintiffs brought suit against defendant insurers, seeking benefits under uninsured motorist provisions of their insurance policies. Under Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3010, which was ultimately repealed, it was mandated that any automobile liability policy issued must include uninsured motorist coverage, unless such coverage was rejected. The court held that uninsured motorist coverage was to be afforded to plaintiffs under all circumstances. The coverage was deemed "portable," meaning that insured and family members were covered at any time, upon being involved in an accident with an uninsured motorist. However, upon repeal of § 500.3010, the purpose of providing uninsured motorist coverage to all insured parties was served by other avenues, including no-fault coverage under Mich. Comp. Laws § 257.1105. Nevertheless, defendants were not permitted to deduct no-fault benefits from the amount otherwise recoverable under uninsured motorist coverage, because this would defeat the reasonable expectations of plaintiffs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, holding that uninsured motorist coverage was to be afforded to plaintiffs under all circumstances, but it reversed that part of the judgment allowing defendants to deduct no-fault benefits from the amount recoverable under uninsured motorist coverage.

Supreme Court of Michigan


409 Mich. 126; 293 N.W.2d 332; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 233


January 8, 1980, Argued

DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:

OVERVIEW: An altercation arose between defendant and the brother of the decedent, who beat defendant with a brick. On the way to the hospital, defendant confronted the decedent about what his brother had done to defendant and shot the decedent. Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder. OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for first-degree murder and remanded the case for a new trial.

January 10, 1980, Argued  
June 6, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 409 Mich 1101.
Rehearing denied by City of Detroit v. Detroit Police Officers Ass'n, 409 Mich. 1101, 1980 Mich. LEXIS 1488 (1980)
Appeal dismissed by Detroit v. Detroit Police Officers Asso., 450 U.S. 903, 101 S. Ct. 1337, 67 L. Ed. 2d 326, 1981 U.S. LEXIS 712 (1981)


PRIOR HISTORY: City of Detroit v. Detroit Police Officers Ass'n, 406 Mich. 985, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 1401 (1979)
City of Detroit v. Detroit Police Officers Ass'n, 407 Mich. 909, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 612 (1979)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed, except as to the hardship exemption from the city's residency requirement.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city sought review of the order of the court of appeals (Michigan) that denied it's motion for a stay of proceedings pending appeal and ordered that the arbitration panel's award, dealing with cost of living allowance (COLA), wages, and a residency hardship exemption be enforced in its entirety. Defendant police and fire unions cross-appealed the denial of interest on the economic awards.


OVERVIEW: An arbitration panel was appointed pursuant to the mandatory arbitration provisions of 1969 Mich. Pub. Acts 312 (act), codified as amended at Mich. Comp. Laws § 423.231 et seq., to resolve the unsettled issues between plaintiff city and defendant police and fire unions. The court denied defendants' request for interest on the economic portion of the award, because there was no statutory basis for such an award.

The court concluded that there was no statutory basis for a grant of interest on mandatory arbitration awards.

Kosa v. State Treasurer


Docket Nos. 60701, 60712


Supreme Court of Michigan


408 Mich. 356; 292 N.W.2d 452; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 224


November 15, 1978, Argued  
May 27, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 78 Mich App 316; 259 NW2d 463 (1977).
Kosa v. Treasurer of State, 78 Mich. App. 316, 259 N.W.2d 463, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1198 (1977)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed in part, but its order of mandamus is vacated because it was rendered moot by the enactment of 1977 PA 275 and the case is remanded to the Court of Appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant retirement system appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals of Michigan which, in an action filed by plaintiff teachers, issued a writ of mandamus ordering the retirement system to cease borrowing funded retirement reserves to meet current unfunded retirement obligations.


OVERVIEW: The teachers filed suit seeking a writ of mandamus to prohibit the retirement system from using current teachers' retirement fund payments to pay retired teachers' monthly retirement benefits. The court held that the Act was not unconstitutional because it did not impair contracts as full pension payments were being or would be timely made. The court remanded the matter for a determination of whether it satisfied the constitutional funding requirements.


OUTCOME: The writ of mandamus was vacated and the case was remanded to determine whether the required funds were appropriated by the new statute or whether there was an actual shortfall.

Council No 11 v. Civil Serv. Comm'n


Docket No. 62418


Supreme Court of Michigan


408 Mich. 385; 292 N.W.2d 442; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 225


November 13, 1979, Argued  
May 27, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 87 Mich App 420; 274 NW2d 804 (1978).
American Federation of State, County & Municipal Employees (AFSCME) v. Michigan Civil Service Com., 87 Mich. App. 420, 274 N.W.2d 804, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2690 (1978)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Civil Service Commission (commission) sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which upheld 1976 Mich. Pub. Acts 169 and struck down Mich. Civ. OVERVIEW: The employees were in the state civil service. One employee was discharged for violating Mich. Civ. Serv. Comm. People v. Gay


Docket Nos. 61202, 60833


Supreme Court of Michigan


407 Mich. 681; 289 N.W.2d 651; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 207


April 3, 1979, Argued  
March 4, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Gay, 402 Mich. 939, 1978 Mich. LEXIS 776 (1978)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed orders granting defendants' original motions for peremptory reversal of their convictions in a state court, for murder during the commission of an armed robbery. Defendants were previously convicted in federal court on similar charges and challenged their state convictions via a remand to the state court based on double jeopardy grounds.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with the federal offenses of bank robbery, assault with a deadly weapon, and taking the life of another while committing a bank robbery. Both defendants were convicted of some or all the charges and were sentenced to federal imprisonment. On similar state charges, both defendants were convicted and sentenced to life imprisonment, and they appealed. On remand to the trial court, the judge found that both defendants were twice placed in jeopardy and dismissed their state convictions. The state sought review of the appellate court's affirmance and the court affirmed, holding that the interest of the state in trying both defendants for murder did not differ substantially from the interest previously addressed in the federal prosecution and thus the subsequent state trial for the same acts violated the Michigan constitutional prohibition against double jeopardy. The court found that a state court decision rendered after defendants' state convictions must be accorded retroactive effect as its purpose in affording protection against double jeopardy was substantive and fundamental and its application did not prejudice the state's administration of justice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed dismissal of defendants' state court convictions on similar charges for which they had already been convicted in federal court.

People v. McLeod


Docket No. 60368


Supreme Court of Michigan


407 Mich. 632; 288 N.W.2d 909; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 206


February 1, 1979, Argued  
March 4, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 77 Mich App 327; 258 NW2d 214 (1977).
People v. McLeod, 77 Mich. App. 327, 258 N.W.2d 214, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1012 (1977)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the "guilty but mentally ill" verdict established by Mich. Comp. Laws § 768.36(1), Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1059(1), following his conviction and sentencing for arson.


OVERVIEW: The trial court declared that defendant would not receive treatment after conviction for arson on a finding of "guilty but mentally ill" under Mich. Comp. Laws § 768.36(3), Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1059(3), and that Mich. Comp. Laws § 768.36(4), Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1059(4), violated equal protection guarantees by mandating a minimum five-year term of probation without regard to the existence or extent of their mental illness or the time needed for treatment. The court of appeals reversed, the trial court re-sentenced defendant, and the court granted defendant leave to appeal. The court agreed with the appellate court that the finding of unconstitutionality was premature because it was based on speculation that the Department of Corrections or the Department of Mental Health would not pay heed to the statute. To have properly made such findings, the trial court should have determined (1) whether treatment was psychiatrically indicated; (2) if so, the type and length of the treatment indicated; (3) whether that treatment was being provided or would be provided; and (4) if not, the reasons for the failure to provide such treatment. There was no violation of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court that reversed the sentence imposed by the trial court.

People v. Wright


Docket No. 60646


Supreme Court of Michigan


408 Mich. 1; 289 N.W.2d 1; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 210


January 10, 1979, Argued  
March 4, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at People v. Wright, 99 Mich. App. 801, 298 N.W.2d 857, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2909 (1980)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 78 Mich App 246; 259 NW2d 443 (1977).
People v. Wright, 78 Mich. App. 246, 259 N.W.2d 443, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1187 (1977)



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part, affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a court of appeals (Michigan) judgment that reversed defendants', one and two, convictions for conspiracy to deliver heroin and delivery of heroin on the basis of the erroneous jury instructions that were given at trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant one and defendant two were charged with and convicted of conspiracy to deliver heroin and delivery of heroin. The court of appeals reversed both defendants' convictions on the basis of certain jury instructions that were given at trial, as those instructions operated to shift the burden of persuasion in the case. On appeal, the court reviewed the challenged instructions and determined that the instructions may have been interpreted by the jury as a burden-shifting presumption, and were therefore unconstitutional. Upon careful examination of the entire record, however, the court concluded that as to defendant one, the erroneous instructions were harmless beyond a reasonable doubt in view of the ample evidence provided in support of his guilt. Thus, his conviction was reinstated. But, the court determined that the erroneous instructions were not harmless as to defendant two, as the state had failed to set forth substantial evidence in support of his guilt for the underlying offenses. Thus, the court affirmed the court of appeals' reversal of his conviction.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals that reversed defendant one's conviction for the charged drug offenses was reversed, and his conviction reinstated. However, the judgment that reversed defendant two's conviction for the same offenses was affirmed. Additionally, both defendants' cases were remanded to the court of appeals for further proceedings.

 

265 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Denham v. Bedford


Docket Nos. 61284, 61283


Supreme Court of Michigan


407 Mich. 517; 287 N.W.2d 168; 1980 Mich. LEXIS 201


June 5, 1979, Argued  
February 1, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 82 Mich App 107; 266 NW2d 682 (1978).
Denham v. Bedford, 82 Mich. App. 107, 266 N.W.2d 682, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2199 (1978)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party filed an action against defendants, insured and insurer, and was awarded damages and prejudgment interest. The trial court held that the insurer was not liable for interest over the policy limit. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed and held that the insurer was liable under Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.6013 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 27A.6013) on that portion of the judgment representing the policy limits. The insurer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The insurer argued that any payment of prejudgment interest would exceed its contractual obligation under the policy. While both the Insurance Code, Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.100 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.1100 et seq.) and the financial responsibility act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 257.501 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 9.2201 et seq.) did purport to govern the practices of insurance companies, there was no indication that the legislature intended that the provisions of those acts be all-inclusive. It would be fundamentally unreasonable to conclude that the legislature intended that the statute apply to individual and corporate defendants, or the self-insured, or the State of Michigan but not to insurance companies. Because there was an obvious conflict between the provisions of the policy which provided for postjudgment interest only and the prejudgment interest statute which provided for interest from the date of filing the complaint, the court held that the boilerplate language amended the insurance policy to conform with the mandate of the prejudgment interest statute. The insurer was liable for prejudgment interest on that portion of the judgment representing its policy liability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the insurer was liable for prejudgment interest on that portion of the judgment representing its policy liability.

268 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Underhill v. Safeco Ins. Co.


Docket Nos. 58567, 60967


Supreme Court of Michigan


407 Mich. 175; 284 N.W.2d 463; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 398


May 30, 1979, Argued  
October 29, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at Porter v. Michigan Mut. Liability Co., 97 Mich. App. 281, 293 N.W.2d 799, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2653 (1980)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 76 Mich App 13; 255 NW2d 349 (1976).

 80 Mich App 145; 263 NW2d 318 (1977).
Porter v. Michigan Mut. Liability Co., 80 Mich. App. 145, 263 N.W.2d 318, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1263 (1977)
Underhill v. Safeco Ins. Co., 76 Mich. App. 13, 255 N.W.2d 349, 1976 Mich. App. LEXIS 649 (1976)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed in Underhill. The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed in Porter as to the constitutionality of the delegation of authority to the Insurance Commissioner to approve deductibles, and the case is remanded to the Court of Appeals for consideration of the additional challenges to the validity of the $ 5,000 deductible.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two cases consolidated for appeal, insurance companies appealed from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which held that they were obligated to pay personal injury protection (no fault) benefits to motorcyclists, who were not required to carry no fault insurance, when motorcyclists were involved in accidents with automobiles which did carry no fault insurance benefits.


OVERVIEW: These two cases involved motorcycles which were involved in accidents with automobiles and presented the question of whether the Michigan no-fault act was constitutional because it permitted motorcyclists to claim no-fault benefits while not requiring them to carry insurance providing such benefits. The lower court held the act constitutional and insurers appealed. The court affirmed, saying that the purpose of the act, and facts concerning automobile/motorcycle accidents justified treating motorcycles like pedestrians as opposed to automobiles. The fact that motorcycles are rarely at fault in such accidents, and that the motorcycle/automobile accident is much like the bicycle/automobile accident, provided a rational basis for the legislative distinction. The court reversed a determination by the Court of Appeals in one case that delegation of authority to the commissioner to determine deductibles was unconstitutional because it lacked standards. The court said that the statute provided sufficient standards to satisfy either a due process test or a safeguard against abuse of discretion test. The court noted also that judicial review of approved deductibles was available.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments in both cases that motorcyclists which were injured in accidents with automobiles were able to claim no-fault benefits even though they were exempt from carrying no-fault insurance, because the statutory scheme was reasonably related to legitimate government interests.

 

269 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Hughes v. Judges' Ret. Bd.


Docket No. 60922


Supreme Court of Michigan


407 Mich. 75; 282 N.W.2d 160; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 391


December 6, 1978, Argued  
August 28, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Hughes v. Judges' Ret. Bd., 402 Mich. 950h, 1978 Mich. LEXIS 1050 (1978)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant retired judges filed an original proceeding for a writ of mandamus to compel appellee Judges' Retirement Board to increase their pension benefits under the Judges' Retirement Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 38.801 et seq., in accord with a 1974 amendment. After the Court of Appeals (Michigan) denied relief, the judges were granted leave to appeal on two specific questions concerning the Act's retrospective application and its constitutionality.


OVERVIEW: The judges sought to compel the Board to grant them increases in their annuities in accord with an amendment to the Act. When the Board refused to do so, the judges sought a writ of mandamus. The court of appeals denied relief but the court granted leave to appeal for the purpose of consideration of the judges' claims that the amendment applied retrospectively and that a refusal to increase their benefits violated the judges' equal protection rights. The court found that the amendment in fact had retrospective application to the extent that years of service earned prior to its enactment were to be counted for the purpose of determining the benefit to which any retiring judge was entitled. However, the court ruled that the statute, as amended, applied only to "members" of the retirement system. Based on the statutory definitions of "member" and "beneficiary," the court held that once the retired judges began to receive annuities or other benefits, they ceased to be "members" and therefore they were not entitled to the increased benefit. The court also rejected an equal protection claim, ruling that the statutory classifications were neither arbitrary nor unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the judges' petition for a writ of mandamus to compel the Board to increase their pension benefits.

 

270 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


In re Request for Advisory Opinion etc.


Docket No. 63476


Supreme Court of Michigan


407 Mich. 60; 281 N.W.2d 322; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 389



July 30, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] See opinion on same request by the Governor, post, 506.



DISPOSITION: Request declined.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Legislature sought an advisory opinion regarding the construction of the Headlee Amendment, Mich.. Const. art. IX, §§ 25-34, regarding state funding of local government programs.


OVERVIEW: The legislature amended the constitution to contain provisions related to state funding of local programs. The legislature sought an advisory opinion regarding the construction of the statute and whether or not it was constitutional. The court declined the request. The court held that the questions were stated in general terms only and the request failed to particularize the claims of unconstitutionality. The court held that the Headlee Amendment was too complex to be interpreted without jurisprudence developed through adversary litigation, especially in light of the provisions permitting taxpayers to contest the amendment, Mich. Const. art. IX, § 32. The court held that the provision in a piece of proposed legislation which required the court to render an opinion as to the Headlee Amendment's constitutionality was improper because it impermissibly involved the court in the legislative process.


OUTCOME: The court denied the legislature's request to give an advisory opinion regarding the construction and constitutionality of a constitutional amendment.

 

271 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


In the Matter of the Petition for a Representation Election Among Supreme Court Staff Employees


Docket No. 62109


Supreme Court of Michigan


406 Mich. 647; 281 N.W.2d 299; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 387; 103 L.R.R.M. 2075


February 1, 1979, Argued  
July 27, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] See order on rehearing of December 6, 1979 withdrawing Justice Ryan's signature from Justice Williams' opinion and substituting a concurrence in Chief Justice Coleman's result, post, 690.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Michigan Employment Relations Commission (MERC) attempted to exercise jurisdiction over the court after petitioner union requested an election among clerical employees of the court. MERC asserted that it had jurisdiction over the court because Mich. Const. art. 3, § 2, the separation of power provision, did not apply, as the clerical employees did not exercise the powers of one branch of government over another.


OVERVIEW: When the union petitioned for an election of the court's clerical employees, the court requested that MERC postpone the election conference and consider whether MERC had jurisdiction over the court. MERC concluded that it had jurisdiction, but the court held that MERC's jurisdiction violated the separation of powers. MERC cited no authority where an executive agency had jurisdiction over the court, and MERC'S jurisdiction could have resulted in the court appealing MERC's decision to a subordinate court. The court rejected MERC's position that the clerical employees were not exercising the powers of one branch of government over another because it was MERC that was attempting jurisdiction, not the court's employees. The court also rejected MERC's contention that it had jurisdiction based on legislation giving MERC authority to adjudicate disputes concerning public employees. The legislation did not specify MERC's jurisdiction over the court, as such an inclusion by the legislature would have violated the separation of powers. Therefore, the normal interpretation of the legislation would have been read as not conferring jurisdiction on the court.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed MERC's case in which the court was a party, holding that MERC did not have jurisdiction over the court.

 

272 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


White v. Ann Arbor


Docket Nos. 60913, 60919


Supreme Court of Michigan


406 Mich. 554; 281 N.W.2d 283; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 380


November 15, 1978, Argued  
July 24, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 80 Mich App 346; 263 NW2d 367 (1977).
White v. Detroit Edison Co., 80 Mich. App. 346, 263 N.W.2d 367, 1977 Mich. App. LEXIS 1293 (1977)



DISPOSITION: Reversed as to White v Ann Arbor. Affirmed as to White v The Detroit Edison Company.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the grant of summary judgment, affirmed by the Court of Appeals (Michigan), to appellee taxpayer in an action asserting that a city ordinance authorizing a cable franchise violated Mich. Const. of 1963, art. 7, § 25. The taxpayer challenged the grant of summary judgment to appellee utility in the taxpayer's action asserting that a cable television franchise could not legally utilize the utility's poles.


OVERVIEW: The court first considered the challenge of the franchise ordinance and discussed the history of Mich. Const. of 1963, art. 7, § 25. Based on that history and the construction applied by the court to predecessor provisions, the court concluded that Mich. Const. of 1963, art. 7, § 25 applied only to utilities supplying water, light, heat, power, or transportation as listed in it and was not, therefore, applicable to cable television franchises. The court then considered application of the Subdivision Control Act of 1967 (Act), Mich. Comp. Laws § 560.101 et seq., which it described as a land use planning statute. The court determined that, although cable television was not identical to telephone service, it was a "similar service" within the meaning of the act and, therefore, was a "public utility" for purposes of the act.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed as to the city and the constitutionality of its ordinance. The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed as to the use of the utility's poles by a cable television franchise.

Wayne County Prosecutor v. Recorder's Court Judge


Docket Nos. 62083, 62084


Supreme Court of Michigan


406 Mich. 374; 280 N.W.2d 793; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 369


January 10, 1979, Argued  
June 25, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied July 18, 1979, post, p 1127. Appeal dismissed by the Supreme Court of the United States November 13, 1979.
Appeal dismissed by Brintley v. Michigan, 444 U.S. 948, 100 S. Ct. 418, 62 L. Ed. 2d 317, 1979 U.S. LEXIS 3704 (1979)
Rehearing denied by Wayne County Prosecutor v. Recorder's Court Judge, 406 Mich. 1127, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 1523 (1979)


PRIOR HISTORY: 85 Mich App 727; 272 NW2d 587 (1978).

 85 Mich App 714; 272 NW2d 582 (1978).
People v. Alexander, 85 Mich. App. 727, 272 N.W.2d 587, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2453 (1978)
People v. Brintley, 85 Mich. App. 714, 272 N.W.2d 582, 1978 Mich. App. LEXIS 2451 (1978)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which determined that Michigan's felony-firearm statute, Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.227b, violated U.S. Const. amend. V and Mich. Const. art. I, § 15, and vacated two defendants' felony-firearm convictions.


OVERVIEW: In one lower court case, the judge refused to sentence a defendant convicted of second-degree murder for the two-year mandatory sentence for felony-firearm. In the other case, a defendant appealed the imposition of § 750.227b on his armed robbery conviction. On appeal, the court affirmed that the legislature intended to create a separate firearm crime that was distinct from a felony or attempted felony, and that cumulative punishments should be imposed. The court noted that the language of § 750.2276b was significantly different from statutes that imposed sentence enhancements or minimum sentences. The statutory elements of the Blockburger test were satisfied by § 750.227b because to obtain a conviction, the prosecution was required to prove that defendants carried a firearm and that the firearm was used during the commission of any felony or attempted felony. The elements of § 750.2276 differed from the elements of the felonies for which defendants were convicted. Defendants were not entitled to an exception to Blockburger because the legislature clearly indicated its intention to impose multiple convictions and cumulative punishments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's holding that the felony-firearm statute was unconstitutional and reinstated one defendant's conviction and sentence for felony-firearm. The court remanded the other defendant to the lower court with instructions to impose a felony-firearm conviction and mandatory two-year sentence.

Ed Zaagman, Inc. v. Kentwood


Docket Nos. 57225, 57342


Supreme Court of Michigan


406 Mich. 137; 277 N.W.2d 475; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 353


October 5, 1976, Argued  
March 27, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 61 Mich App 693; 233 NW2d 146 (1975).

 61 Mich App 435; 232 NW2d 732 (1975).
Ed Zaagman, Inc. v. Kentwood, 61 Mich. App. 693, 233 N.W.2d 146, 1975 Mich. App. LEXIS 1585 (1975)
Turkish v. Warren, 61 Mich. App. 435, 232 N.W.2d 732, 1975 Mich. App. LEXIS 1547 (1975)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in Zaagman. Affirmed, as modified, and remanded in Turkish.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the trial court's finding that defendant city's single-family zoning ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to plaintiff landowners, was challenged.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landowners brought an action against defendant city challenging the validity of the city's single-family zoning ordinance. The trial court and the court below held that the city's zoning designation was unreasonable, arbitrary, and confiscatory and therefore constitutionally invalid. The appellate court agreed and found that defendant's platting of surrounding land had landlocked plaintiffs' parcel to the extent that all reasonable uses were precluded under the single-family zoning designation. Plaintiffs affirmatively proved that their development costs under the disputed ordinance would be roughly equivalent to any potential sales returns on the developed property, thereby rendering the classification scheme unreasonable and void. But after considering several alternatives, the court concluded that the matter should be remanded to the zoning authority to present for consideration an adopted amendatory ordinance comporting with the dictates of equity as well as the requirements of constitutional reasonableness as applied to plaintiffs' parcel. The judgment was affirmed in part, modified in part, and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the courts below, finding defendant city's single-family zoning ordinance unreasonable, arbitrary and confiscatory because it interfered with plaintiff property owners making any beneficial use of their property.

 

275 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Michigan Oil Co. v. Natural Resources Com.


Docket No. 59088


Supreme Court of Michigan


406 Mich. 1; 276 N.W.2d 141; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 348; 62 Oil & Gas Rep. 313; 9 ELR 20625


May 3, 1978, Argued  
March 1, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied, May 7, 1979 post, p 1121. Certiorari Denied by the Supreme Court of the United States December 3, 1979.
Rehearing denied by Mich. Oil Co. v. Natural Res. Comm'n, 406 Mich. 1121, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 1537 (1979)
Writ of certiorari denied Michigan Oil Co. v. Natural Resources Com., 444 U.S. 980, 100 S. Ct. 482, 62 L. Ed. 2d 407, 1979 U.S. LEXIS 3966 (1979)


PRIOR HISTORY: 71 Mich App 667; 249 NW2d 135 (1976).
Michigan Oil Co. v. Natural Resources Com., 71 Mich. App. 667, 249 N.W.2d 135, 1976 Mich. App. LEXIS 996 (1976)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff drilling company sought review of the judgment from the court of appeals (Michigan), which denied plaintiff a drilling permit pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 319.1 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 13.139(1)). The issue presented was whether the oil conservation act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 319.1 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 13.139(1)) provided sufficient authority to justify the denial of the permit.


OVERVIEW: Following the judgment denying plaintiff drilling company a drilling permit pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 319.1 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 13.139(1)), plaintiff appealed. Plaintiff contended that § 319.1 only empowered defendant commission to withhold issuance of a drilling permit to prohibit waste, which was unnecessary, to the production of oil and gas. The court affirmed, holding that § 319.1 provided sufficient authority to justify the denial of the permit. Further, the court found that the oil conservation act placed an affirmative duty on defendant to prevent waste, including serious or unnecessary damage to or destruction of wildlife, even in the absence of specifically promulgated rules and regulations and that the proposed drilling posed a serious threat to the survival of wildlife. The court reasoned that the serious threat fell within the statutory definitions of ordinary waste and surface waste, which included the unnecessary damage to, or destruction of animal life. The court concluded that any other finding would have seriously undermined the duty to protect and conserve the natural resources of the state.


OUTCOME: The judgment that denied plaintiff drilling company a permit to drill oil and gas pursuant to the oil conservation act was affirmed because defendant commission acted within its authority in denying the permit. The court reasoned that the oil conservation act placed an affirmative duty on defendant to prevent waste and the serious threat to wildlife that the drilling posed fell within the statutory definition of ordinary and surface waste.

 

276 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Bush v. Oscoda Area Schools


Docket No. 59242


Supreme Court of Michigan


405 Mich. 716; 275 N.W.2d 268; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 346


March 7, 1978, Argued  
February 9, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Bush v. Oscoda Area Sch., 407 Mich. 1155, 1979 Mich. LEXIS 463 (1979)
Decision reached on appeal by Bush v. Oscoda Area Schools, 109 Mich. App. 373, 311 N.W.2d 788, 1981 Mich. App. LEXIS 3277 (1981)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 72 Mich App 670; 250 NW2d 759 (1976).
Bush v. Oscoda Area Schools, 72 Mich. App. 670, 250 N.W.2d 759, 1976 Mich. App. LEXIS 1134 (1976)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff parent appealed the order of the Michigan Court of Appeals that affirmed summary judgment granted to defendants, the school district and the superintendent, in the parent's negligence action to recover for injury incurred by her daughter in a fire while clearing up after a science experiment in a converted school laboratory. The Court of Appeals also reversed the grant of summary judgment to defendants, principal and teacher.


OVERVIEW: The class attended by the parent's daughter was held in a non-laboratory used as lab because of space constraints. Unlike labs, the room was unequipped with safety shower, ventilation or exhaust hoods, sinks, enclosed storage areas, stationary lab desks, or water and gas outlets. The daughter was attempting to extinguish a lighted burner when the explosion occurred. The school district argued that the statutory grant of governmental immunity allowed liability for injuries resulting from defective conditions in public buildings caused by inadequate or negligent repairs or maintenance only. The court disagreed and reversed the summary judgment awarded to the school district and the superintendent because the case fell within the dangerous building exception to governmental immunity. In utilizing the room as a laboratory, the school district was required to take actions reasonably necessary to protect the students. Further, a building could be defective due to improper design, faulty construction, or the absence of safety devices. Summary judgment was not appropriate because it was a question of fact as to significance of the defect in relation to the daughter's injuries.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's affirmance of the summary judgment granted in favor of the school district and the superintendent and remanded the cause for trial.

 

277 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Alford


Docket No. 59557


Supreme Court of Michigan


405 Mich. 570; 275 N.W.2d 484; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 342


March 8, 1978, Argued  
February 8, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 73 Mich App 604; 251 NW2d 314 (1977).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed the trial court's grant of defendant's motion to quash the information, which charged him with two counts of unlawful delivery of a controlled substance, in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 335.341(1)(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with two counts of unlawful delivery of a controlled substance, in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 335.341(1)(b), for giving prescriptions for 120 capsules containing amphetamines to an undercover police officer in the names of persons never examined by the doctor and for actually giving 103 capsules containing barbiturates to the same undercover police officer. The trial court granted a pretrial motion to quash the information. The appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court and held that physicians not acting in good faith in the course of professional practice or research were prosecutable under Mich. Comp. Laws § 335.341(1); Mich. Stat. Ann. § 18.1070(41)(1). Whether a physician was acting in good faith in the course of professional practice or research was a question of fact. The court also held that the defense of entrapment was not established as a matter of law. The undercover police officer merely presented himself at the doctor's office and asked for drugs, which he received. His actions were not so reprehensible that they should be excluded from the judicial administration of criminal justice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court, which reversed the trial court's decision quashing the information against defendant charging two counts of unlawful delivery of a controlled substance.

 

278 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


Docket No. 56620


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


405 Mich. 418; 275 N.W.2d 466; 1979 Mich. LEXIS 332


March 7, 1978, Argued  
February 5, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 57 Mich App 556; 226 NW2d 673 (1975).
People v. Smith, 57 Mich. App. 556, 226 N.W.2d 673, 1975 Mich. App. LEXIS 1628 (1975)



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the courts below are reversed, the conviction vacated, and the defendant discharged forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for second-degree murder obtained by plaintiff state in Kalamazoo Circuit Court (Michigan). Defendant claimed that § 8 of the Criminal Sexual Psychopath Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 780.501-780.509 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.967(1)-28.967(9)), prohibited the state from trying him for murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder and found to be a criminal sexual psychopathic under the Criminal Sexual Psychopath Act (CSA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 780.501-780.509 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.967(1)-28.967(9)). Defendant spent the next 18 years confined in the state hospital, during which time, § 780.501-780.509 was repealed. Defendant was released after the statute was repealed and the state then revived the murder charge against defendant, which resulted in his ultimate conviction. Defendant claimed that § 8 bar of prosecution in the CSA prohibited the state from trying him on the murder charge. On appeal, the court found that the extraordinary circumstances of the case warranted a departure from the general rule of total ab initio nullity applied in the lower courts. The court noted that the state erroneously relied upon the premises that since the act was a nullity from its inception, the state's assurance to defendant that he would never be tried was likewise a nullity. The court stated that such a position did not comport with traditional notions of fundamental fairness or principles of fundamental human justice.


OUTCOME: The court ruled against the state and reversed the decisions of the lower courts. The court vacated defendant's conviction for second degree murder.
